Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the temporality of an entity is not sufficient to prove its creation.
Correction: Maturidi affirms that temporality is a convincing proof for creation, and the people of Tawhid rely on it without needing additional arguments.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the proof of the creation of a body?
A) Its motion
B) Its temporality
C) Its accidents
D) Its partition

Key Takeaway
The temporality of bodies is the fundamental proof for their creation, and alternative arguments such as from accidents or partition are insufficient or problematic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly rest your faith on the simple fact that everything temporal points to a Creator, or do you secretly demand more 'proof' before your heart submits? What does that reveal about your reliance on Allah?
?
Maturidi warns against arguments that become a source of doubt rather than certainty. Have you ever allowed intellectual discussions about faith to create pride or confusion, rather than deepening your humble acknowledgment that your very existence is proof enough? How will you guard against that?
?
If you believe that the temporality of your own body is sufficient proof that you are created by Allah, why do you live as if this world is permanent? Look at your attachments, your fears, your hopes—are they anchored in the temporal or the eternal? What must change today?

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate
5 min
Al-Ka'bi's Claim: Command Requires Preceding Power
Al-Ka'bi's claim about command and power timing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Ka'bi's argument that the command to believe implies a power that existed before the state of disbelief.

Core Idea
Al-Ka'bi ties divine command to power that must precede, not accompany.

In Islamic theology, the relationship between divine command and human power is debated. Al-Ka'bi presents a view that the command to believe implies a power that existed before disbelief. This lesson unpacks his reasoning and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Ka'bi's claim
Read the first sentence of the Arabic passage carefully. It states that the disbeliever is commanded to believe in his state of disbelief.
This is the starting point of the theological discussion.
2
Understand his interpretation
Al-Ka'bi interprets the command as implying a preceding prohibition, which necessitates a preceding power. Grasp the logical chain: command → prohibition → preceding power.
This is a key move in his argument.
3
Note the two possibilities
Al-Ka'bi mentions two possibilities: either he left the first opinion (that the power preceded) to align with Muslims or he directed to what was possible; in the other case he did not speak. Reflect on the ambiguity.
This reveals inconsistency in his position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم أن الكافر مأمور في حال كفره بالإيمان. تأويله أن النهي تقدمه، فيلزمه أن يقول: هو قادر عليه بقدرة تقدمت وزعم أنه ترك في الأول لقول المسلمين ووجه إلى ما أمكن، وفي الآخر لم يقل .
نقول نحن وبالله التوفيق لا أحد من المسلمين إلا وعنده أن الكافر في حال كفره قوي على ما هو عليه ; فقل في القدرة مثل الذي قلت في الأمر، إذ المعنى واحد في القول والتحصيل
And he claimed that the disbeliever is commanded in the state of his disbelief to have faith. His interpretation is that the prohibition preceded him, so it necessitates that he say: he is capable of it by a power that preceded. And he claimed that he left in the first for the saying of the Muslims and directed to what was possible, and in the other he did not say. We say: and by God is success—there is none among the Muslims except that he holds that the disbeliever in the state of his disbelief is strong over what he is upon; so say concerning power the like of what you said concerning the command, for the meaning is one in statement and realization.

Commentary
This passage deals with the issue of whether the disbeliever is commanded to believe while he is in a state of disbelief. Al-Ka'bi claims that the command implies a preceding prohibition, and thus the disbeliever must have a power (qudra) that preceded his disbelief. Maturidi responds that all Muslims agree that the disbeliever has power (quwa) over what he is upon (i.e., his disbelief). So Maturidi argues that the same principle applies: just as the command is given, so is the power present. The meaning is identical in both statement and realization. In other words, the disbeliever has the capacity to believe (or to continue in disbelief) at the moment of the command, and this does not require a preceding power.

Key Takeaway
Al-Ka'bi argues that the command to believe in a state of disbelief requires a power that existed prior to that state.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

How might you instead take responsibility for the ability you once had but wasted?
?
If al-Ka'bi's reasoning is a subtle evasion of present accountability, what is one command you have been avoiding today by telling yourself you are 'not ready'? What would it look like to act as if the ability to obey is already with you, right now?
?
Al-Ka'bi's argument shifts the power to obey into the past, making present failure seem inevitable. Is there a sin you have normalized by thinking your capacity to change is gone? What would it mean to trust that Allah's command itself proves you can obey—not because of a past power, but because He commands you now?

Refuting the Claim of Command-in-Disbelief
Maturidi refutes the claim that the disbeliever is commanded to believe while disbelieving.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the disbeliever cannot be under a command to believe simultaneously with his disbelief, and why this would invalidate the entire structure of divine command and prohibition.

Core Idea
A command to believe in the moment of disbelief destroys the meaning of command.

Maturidi engages with a subtle theological challenge: if the disbeliever is commanded to believe while persisting in disbelief, then the command becomes paradoxical. In this passage, he turns the opponent's reasoning against them, showing that such a view leads to the complete nullification of all divine commands. Let's examine how he builds this argument.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
Read the first sentence to understand what the opponent argues: that the disbeliever is commanded (مأمور) to believe while still in a state of disbelief. Note how they interpret the command as preceded by a prohibition.
The opponent tries to reconcile command and disbelief by appealing to time.
2
Follow Maturidi's initial response on power
Maturidi first points out that all Muslims agree the disbeliever has power (قوة) over his disbelief. He then challenges the opponent to apply their same reasoning to power as they did to command.
This turns the opponent's method against them.
3
Trace the reductio ad absurdum
Maturidi shows that if the disbeliever is neither commanded nor prohibited at the moment of disbelief, then this repeats in every moment, leading to the nullification of all command and prohibition forever.
This is the core of the refutation: the consequence is far-fetched (بعيد).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وزعم أن الكافر مأمور في حال كفره بالإيمان. تأويله أن النهي تقدمه، فيلزمه أن يقول: هو قادر عليه بقدرة تقدمت. وزعم أنه ترك في الأول لقول المسلمين ووجه إلى ما أمكن، وفي الآخر لم يقل.

نقول نحن وبالله التوفيق: لا أحد من المسلمين إلا وعنده أن الكافر في حال كفره قوي على ما هو عليه؛ فقل في القدرة مثل الذي قلت في الأمر، إذ المعنى واحد في القول والتحصيل جميعا. ثم تأويله قول المسلمين على وجه يعلم كل مسلم أن ذلك لم يخطر بباله، بل لا يحتمله عقل كل أحد لو ألزم بجهد: أن يكون كافر ليس بمنهى عن كفره، وليس بمأمور في حاله. فإذا لم يكن في وقته منهيًا مأموراً لما هو فيه ولا لضده، وهو كذلك في الوقت الثاني والثالث إلى ما لا نهاية له؛ وفي ذلك بطلان الأمر والنهي على التحقيق، لأنه يكون الأمر بالشيء للوقت الثاني والنهي عن ضده، وهو في ذلك ليس بمؤتمر بالأمر ولا مرتكب النهي، لأنه ليس في ذلك؛ وكذا في كل وقت، فيبطل حق الأمر والنهي عن الفعل أبدا، ويرجع إلى غير حال الائتمار والارتكاب، وذلك بعيد.
And he claimed that the disbeliever is commanded in his state of disbelief to believe. His interpretation is that prohibition preceded, so he must say: he is capable of it by a power that preceded. And he claimed that he left (that?) in the first for the statement of the Muslims and directed to what was possible, and in the other he did not say.

We say, and with Allah is success: There is no Muslim who does not hold that the disbeliever in his state of disbelief is powerful over what he is upon. So say concerning power similar to what you said concerning command, for the meaning is one in statement and realization. Then his interpretation of the Muslims' statement is in a manner that every Muslim knows that it never occurred to his mind, rather no one's intellect can bear it even if exerted with effort: that a disbeliever is not prohibited from his disbelief and not commanded in his state. So if he is not in his time prohibited nor commanded regarding what he is upon nor its opposite, and likewise in the second time and the third ad infinitum, then that entails the nullification of command and prohibition truly, because the command for something in the second time and the prohibition of its opposite, while he is not in that time obeying the command nor committing the prohibition, because he is not in that (state). And so in every time, thus the right of command and prohibition regarding the act is invalidated forever, and it returns to a state other than that of obedience and commission, and that is far-fetched.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the opponent's claim that the disbeliever is commanded to believe while in his state of disbelief, with the opponent interpreting that the prohibition of disbelief preceded the command. Maturidi first points out the universal Muslim agreement that the disbeliever has power (قوة) over his disbelief; then he turns the opponent's method back on him: if you apply the same reasoning to power as to command, the same absurdity follows. The opponent's interpretation of the Muslim consensus is shown to be not only baseless but also rationally impossible: if the disbeliever is not prohibited from his disbelief and not commanded to believe at the same time, then this situation repeats in every moment, resulting in the complete nullification of all divine commands and prohibitions. The argument concludes that this consequence is far-fetched (بعيد), thus refuting the opponent's claim.

Key Terms
كافر
أمر
نهي
قدرة
إيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Maturidi denies that the disbeliever is ever commanded to believe.
Correction: Maturidi refutes only the idea that the command exists in the very moment of disbelief, not the obligation at other times.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi argue is the consequence of the claim that the disbeliever is commanded in his state of disbelief?
A) It proves the disbeliever is excused
B) It nullifies all command and prohibition
C) It shows the disbeliever has no power
D) It confirms the opponent's view

Key Takeaway
Maturidi refutes the idea that a disbeliever is commanded to believe in the instant of disbelief, as it would render the entire system of divine command and prohibition meaningless.

The disbeliever is powerful over his disbelief. What power do you have over your own persistent sin? Are you using that power to remain in sin or to turn to obedience? Reflect on one sin you feel stuck in—does this lesson challenge your excuse that you 'can't' change?

Willing Disbelief: A Nuanced Perspective
Maturidi explains that willing an act of kufr is not blameworthy when considered in terms of its magnitude.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why willing an act of disbelief is not always blameworthy, and how Maturidi distinguishes between willing the act and willing its magnitude.

Core Idea
The will can be directed at an act's greatness without endorsing its evil.

Imam Maturidi presents a subtle dialectical argument to show that the moral evaluation of a will depends on what aspect of the act is intended. He asks a series of questions to reveal that willing an act of cursing the Prophet can be praiseworthy if one wills it to be a great sin (which it is) rather than willing the evil itself. This distinction guards against simplistic condemnations of all willing of evil.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage Carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Let the rhythm of the dialectic come through.
Notice the repeated phrase 'لا بد من بلى' (it must be yes).
2
Follow the First Question
The first scenario: wanting the cursing of the Prophet to be like that of Iblis in rank and sin. The answer is that such a person is a transgressing fool and disbeliever.
This establishes that some intentions are blameworthy.
3
Follow the Second Question
The second scenario: wanting the cursing of the Prophet to be a great matter, greater than any creature's cursing. The answer is that such a person is praiseworthy.
This shows that the same act can be willed with a different intention.
4
Understand the Conclusion
From the answers, Maturidi infers that the one who wants the cursing to be great must be a disbeliever (since a Muslim cannot will it), and that willing disbelief is not always blameworthy—it depends on the respect (وجه) in which it is willed.
The key phrase: 'جواز إرادة فعل الكفر من وجه لا يحتمل الذم من ذلك الوجه'.
5
Reflect on the Nuance
Consider how this argument guards against overgeneralizing that all willing of evil is blameworthy. The intention matters: is it directed at the act's magnitude or at the evil itself?
This is a lesson in precise moral reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نسأل عمن يريد أن يكون شتم رسول الله مثل شتم إبليس في المرتبة والمأثم أليس هو بمتعد سفه كافر؟ لا بد من بلى. فيقال: من أراد أن يكون شتم رسول الله أمراً عظيماً لا يبلغه شتم أحد من الخلق أليس حقه أن يكون محموداً؟ لا بد من بلى. قيل: فمن يريد أن يكون الشتم منه كذلك، إذ محال كونه لا من أحد مسلم أعظم ولا أصغر ولا نحو ذلك؟ فلا بد أن يقول: من كافر. وفي ذلك جواز إرادة فعل الكفر من وجه لا يحتمل الذم من ذلك الوجه. وبالله التوفيق.
Then we ask about one who wants the cursing of the Messenger of God to be like the cursing of Iblis in rank and sin, is he not transgressing, foolish, an unbeliever? It must be yes. So it is said: The one who wants the cursing of the Messenger of God to be a great matter that the cursing of any creature does not reach, does he not deserve to be praised? It must be yes. It was said: So the one who wants the cursing from him to be thus, since it is impossible that it be from a Muslim greater or smaller or the like? Then he must say: from an unbeliever. And in that is the permissibility of willing the act of disbelief in one respect that does not entail blame from that respect. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage uses a dialectical method to show that willing an act of kufr (disbelief) can be permissible in a certain respect, without blame. It begins by asking about someone who wants the act of cursing the Prophet to be as great a sin as Iblis's cursing: that person would be a transgressing fool and disbeliever (kāfir). Then it contrasts this with someone who wants the cursing of the Prophet to be a great sin (which it is), and thus is praiseworthy. The question then arises: who is it that wants the cursing to be as great as possible? Since it cannot be from a Muslim, it must be from a disbeliever. From this, Maturidi concludes that willing an act of disbelief is not always blameworthy; it can be permissible if the will is directed at the act's magnitude or some other aspect, not the act itself as evil. This is a nuanced point that guards against overgeneralizing that all willing of evil is blameworthy.

Key Terms
الشتم
الكفر
الذم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that willing an act of disbelief is always blameworthy and that Maturidi's argument justifies evil intentions.
Correction: Maturidi does not justify willing evil; he shows that the will can be directed at an attribute (greatness) without endorsing the evil nature of the act. The permissibility is only in that specific respect.

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the ruling on willing the act of cursing the Prophet if one intends it as a great sin?
A) It is always sinful.
B) It is permissible in one respect and not blameworthy.
C) It is only permissible for a Muslim.
D) It is impossible.

Key Takeaway
Willing an act of disbelief can be permissible if the will is directed at the magnitude of the act rather than its evil nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself admiring the sheer magnitude of a sin without any desire to commit it? How does this passage force you to re-examine the object of your admiration and its moral implications?

When you witness a great evil in the world, do you ever feel a kind of awe at its scale? In light of this lesson, what does that awe reveal about your heart's orientation towards the forbidden?
?
The passage distinguishes between intending an act and intending its greatness. Are there areas in your life where you have confused the two, perhaps by 'appreciating' the magnitude of a sin while convincing yourself you are not complicit? How will you apply this distinction starting today?

The Blameworthiness of Willing Insult: A Dialectical Analysis
Maturidi argues that willing the insult as a great act is praiseworthy, but willing it from oneself is blameworthy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi distinguishes between different intentions regarding the insult of the Prophet and how blame is assigned based on the aspect of the will.

Core Idea
Intentions are judged by their object and relation to the agent, not by the act alone.

Maturidi uses a series of rhetorical questions to clarify a subtle point: willing an evil act can be praiseworthy if one intends the act's magnitude as evil, but blameworthy if one intends to perform it. This passage challenges simplistic views of intention and sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the three questions Maturidi poses and the expected answers.
2
Analyze the first question
Identify the first scenario: wanting the insult to be like Iblis's insult. Note the judgment: transgressor, fool, unbeliever.
This sets the baseline for blame.
3
Contrast the second question
Now the scenario changes: wanting the insult to be a great matter. The judgment is praise, not blame. What changed? The object of the will.
The agent desires the evaluation of the act, not the act itself.
4
Examine the third question
Finally, wanting the insult to come from oneself. The judgment returns to unbelief. Why? Because one cannot be a Muslim and will to insult the Prophet.
The impossibility of the act coming from a Muslim.
5
Apply the conclusion
Maturidi's conclusion: willing an act of disbelief from a certain aspect can be free from blame. Understand the principle of considering the aspect of intention.
This is a key nuance in Islamic theology of intention and responsibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم نسأل عمن يريد أن يكون شتم رسول الله مثل شتم إبليس في المرتبة والمأثم أليس هو بمتعد سفيه كافر؟ لا بد من بلى. فيقال: من أراد أن يكون شتم رسول الله أمرا عظيما لا يبلغه شتم أحد من الخلق [أليس حقه أن يكون محمودا؟ لا بد من بلى. قيل: فمن يريد أن يكون الشتم منه كذلك، إذ محال كونه لا من أحد [مسلم] أعظم ولا أصغر ولا نحو ذلك؟ فلا بد أن يقول: من كافر. وفي ذلك جواز إرادة فعل الكفر من وجه لا يحتمل الذم من ذلك الوجه. وبالله التوفيق.
Then we ask about one who wants the insult of the Messenger of God to be like the insult of Iblis in rank and guilt: is he not a transgressor, a fool, an unbeliever? It must be 'yes'. So it is said: Whoever wants the insult of the Messenger of God to be a great matter that the insult of none of creation can reach—[is it not right that he should be praised?] It must be 'yes'. It is said: Then whoever wants the insult to be from himself likewise—since it is impossible for it to be from anyone [Muslim] greater or lesser or the like—then he must say: an unbeliever. And in that is the permissibility of willing the act of disbelief from an aspect that does not admit blame from that aspect. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical argument structured as a series of questions and expected answers. Maturidi first asks about someone who desires that the insult of the Prophet be considered as grave as Iblis's insult: such a person is deemed a transgressor, fool, and unbeliever. Then he contrasts: someone who desires the insult to be a great evil (as a type of action) deserves praise, because he esteems the Prophet highly. Finally, if someone desires the insult to come from himself—since it cannot come from a Muslim—he is guilty of unbelief. The key conclusion: willing an act of disbelief can be free from blame when considered from the aspect of wanting the act to be recognized as evil, not from the aspect of performing it. Thus the intention is evaluated by its object and relation to the agent.

Key Terms
شتم
إرادة
كفر
ذم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any intention to will something evil is sinful regardless of context.
Correction: Maturidi distinguishes between desiring the evil deed as one's own act versus desiring the recognition of its evilness. The latter can be praiseworthy.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the ruling for one who wants the insult of the Prophet to be a great matter?
A) He is an unbeliever
B) He is praiseworthy
C) He is a fool
D) He is a transgressor

Key Takeaway
Intentions are not simply good or bad; their moral status depends on what the person intends and their relation to the act.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you hear someone insult the Prophet (SAW), what is the first desire that arises in your heart? Is it to see the act punished, or to see it recognized as a great evil? Which aspect does your intention reflect, and are you willing to examine it honestly before Allah?
?
Have you ever found yourself wishing ill upon someone else, perhaps thinking it would be justice? How does Maturidi's distinction between wanting the evil itself and wanting its recognition as evil force you to re-evaluate the hidden intentions of your own heart?
?
This lesson shows that even a desire for disbelief can be free of blame from one aspect. What does this reveal about how easily the heart can deceive itself? Ask yourself: have you ever justified a sinful wish by telling yourself it is about the principle? Will you now commit to examining every intention by its true relation to you, not just its object?

The Debate on Sins and Faith
Maturidi presents the view that all sins remove from faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that all sins remove a person from faith according to some scholars, and how they support it with Quranic verses.

Core Idea
All sins are alike in removing faith according to one school.

Maturidi begins this chapter by presenting a controversial view: that all sins, by the name of disobedience, lead to removal from faith. He cites Quranic verses that threaten eternal punishment for disobedience. This sets the stage for the nuanced discussion that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying the group's claim
Read the Arabic text and translation to see how Maturidi introduces the view that all sins remove from faith.
Notice that he uses the term 'قوم' (a group) to indicate this is not necessarily his own view.
2
Examine the Quranic proofs
Look at the two verses cited: 'Wa man ya'si Allaha wa rasoolahu...' and 'Wa ma kana limu'minin wa la mu'minatin...' Reflect on how these verses support the claim that sin leads to removal from faith.
The second verse implies that after God decides, there is no choice left for believers, so disobedience contradicts that.
3
Understand the logical extension
Maturidi states that all sins are one in realizing the name of disobedience, and similarly in realizing going astray and eternal damnation. Consider why he uses the phrase 'تحقيق اسم' (realization of the name).
This is a semantic argument: if the name applies, the ruling applies.

في محل الذنوب وتسمية مقتر فيها ] !
{ قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : } تكلم الناس في محل الذنوب وتسمية مقترفيها. ۱ - فجمع بينها قوم في الإخراج من الإيمان بقوله : ومن يعص الله ورسوله " ، الآية " ، وقوله : وماكان لمؤمن ولا مؤمنة . والذنوب كلها في تحقيق اسم العصيان واحد، فعلى ذلك في تحقيق اسم الضلل وإيجاب الخلود [في النار واحد]. وقوله تعالى: إن تجتنبوا كبائر ما تنهون عنه الله يخرج على وجهين أحدهما أن يُكفّر بالتوبة، لقوله : ويَخْلُدْ فيه مهانًا إلا من تاب الآية، وقوله تعالى : يا أيها الذين / آمنوا توبوا إلى الله توبة نصوحا عسى [١٦٩ظ] ربكم أن يكفّر عنكم سيئاتكم ﴾ [التحريم، ٨/٦٦ ] ، وغير ذلك من الآيات. والثاني أن تكون الصغائر منها التي تقع على السهو والغفلة، فهي المغفورة، بما قال تعالى : ولا يؤاخذكم الله
The Fourth Chapter: Issues of the Major Sin and Its Committer. A question concerning the station of sins and the naming of their committer. Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: People have spoken about the station of sins and the naming of their committers. A group combined them regarding removal from faith by His saying: 'And whoever disobeys God and His Messenger...' the verse, and His saying: 'It is not for a believing man or a believing woman...' (33:36). And all sins in the realization of the name of disobedience are one; likewise in the realization of the name of going astray and the obligation of eternity in the Fire. And His saying: 'If you avoid the major sins (kaba'ir) which you are forbidden...' is subject to two aspects: one, that they are expiated by repentance, by His saying: 'And he will abide therein disgraced, except those who repent...' (25:69-70), and His saying: 'O you who have believed, repent to God with sincere repentance; perhaps your Lord will remove from you your misdeeds...' (66:8), and other verses. The second aspect is that the minor sins (sagha'ir) are those that occur out of forgetfulness and heedlessness; these are forgiven, as He said: 'And God will not take you to account...'
Source pages p. 413

Commentary
This passage introduces the fourth chapter on major sins (kaba'ir) and the status of their committers. Maturidi begins by noting that scholars have differed on the 'place' of sins—meaning their effect on faith—and on what to call those who commit them. He first presents the view of those who argue that all sins, under the name 'disobedience', remove one from faith, citing Quranic verses that threaten eternal punishment. They hold that every sin entails going astray and leads to eternal damnation. Then Maturidi explains that the verse about avoiding major sins (Qur'an 4:31) can be understood in two ways. The first is that major sins are expiated through sincere repentance, supported by verses promising forgiveness to those who repent. The second interpretation is that minor sins (sagha'ir), committed out of forgetfulness or carelessness, are forgiven by God's grace. He begins to cite a verse but the passage cuts off. Thus, Maturidi is setting up the framework for discussing whether sins lead to eternal punishment or not, and how repentance and smaller sins are handled.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All sins are equal and automatically lead to eternal damnation without exception.
Correction: Maturidi presents this view but will later introduce qualifications like repentance and minor sins.

A) The verse 'If you avoid the major sins'

C) The verse 'Repent to God with sincere repentance'
D) The verse 'God will not take you to account for the heedless'

Key Takeaway
Some early scholars argued that all sins, being disobedience, remove a person from faith and lead to eternal punishment.

?

?

?
The passage presents a view that all sins are equal in disobedience. Is there a sin in your life that you have been downplaying? What step will you take today to repent from it and restore your faith?

Two Interpretations of the Verse on Avoiding Major Sins
Maturidi explains two ways to interpret the verse about avoiding major sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's two explanations of the Quranic command to avoid major sins, and how they relate to repentance and minor sins.

Core Idea
Major sins can be expiated by repentance; minor sins of forgetfulness are forgiven.

After presenting the strict view, Maturidi turns to a key verse that seems to offer a more merciful perspective: 'If you avoid the major sins which you are forbidden, We will remove from you your minor misdeeds...'. He explains that this verse has two possible interpretations, each dealing with how sins are expiated or forgiven.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two aspects
Read the passage and locate the two aspects Maturidi presents. The first is about repentance expiating major sins; the second is about minor sins committed through forgetfulness.
Maturidi uses 'أحدهما' and 'والثاني' to clearly separate them.
2
Analyze the Quranic support for each
For the first aspect, he cites verses about repentance. For the second, he begins to cite a verse about not being taken to account for heedless oaths. Consider how each set of verses supports the interpretation.
The second citation is incomplete in our passage.
3
Understand the implications
Think about how these two aspects provide a balanced view of divine justice: major sins are not automatically fatal if repented, and minor sins are treated with leniency.
This contrasts with the earlier strict view.

Two Proofs for Calling the Sinner a Kafir
Maturidi presents two arguments of those who deem the sinner a kafir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two scriptural arguments used by some scholars to justify calling a major sinner a kafir, as reported by Imam Maturidi.

Core Idea
Sin breaks the covenant of faith and incurs punishment reserved for the ungrateful.

Maturidi here reports a contested view: some scholars argued that anyone guilty of a major sin deserves the name 'kafir' (unbeliever). He presents two proofs based on Quranic verses and theological reasoning. Understanding these arguments is essential for grasping the broader debate on faith and works in Islamic theology, and for seeing how Maturidi will later refute this position.

Guided Path
1
Know the context
Recognize that this passage is a report of a specific group's opinion (قوم), not necessarily Maturidi's own view. He will respond to it later.
This prevents misunderstanding the author's position.
2
Examine the first proof
Read the Quranic verses cited (Al-Layl 92:15-16, Saba 34:17, An-Nisa 4:123, Az-Zalzala 99:8) and note how they link punishment to ingratitude and harming the Messenger. The argument: if only the ungrateful (kafur) are punished, and sinners are punished, then sinners are ungrateful and thus kafir.
The verse on harming the Messenger extends the logic to all disobedience.
3
Examine the second proof
Consider the argument from the covenant of faith: a believer's covenant is not to disobey Allah. Sin breaks this covenant, and trials in this world reveal the person's true state, as in the verses about hypocrisy. The sinner's disobedience shows he follows the devil, which is tantamount to worship, and therefore he is a kafir.
This proof uses the idea that actions reveal hidden disbelief.
4
Reflect on the implications
Notice that this position equates major sin with kufr. This was a major debate in early kalam. Understanding these proofs prepares you for the refutation and for the broader discussion on iman and 'amal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم حقق قوم منهم له اسم الكفر بوجهين أحدهما بقوله : ولا يصلاها إلا الأشقى الذي كذب وتولى ﴾ [الليل، ١٥/٩٢-١٦]، وقال : وهل نجازى إلا الكفور ﴾ [سبأ، ١٧/٣٤]، وقال: ومن يعمل سوءا يُجز به ﴾ [النساء، ١٢٣/٤] ، وقال: ومن جاء بالسيئة فلا يُجزى إلا مثلها، وقال : ومن يعمل مثقال ذرة شراً يره ﴾ [الزلزال، ۸/۹۹ ]؛ فأثبت الجزاء فيما صغر منه، وأخبر أنه لا يجازي إلا الكفور ولا يصلاها إلا من ذكر مع ما قال الله تعالى : إن الذين يؤذون الله ورسوله الآية ، وكل عاص فهو يؤذى رسول الله . ولا قوة إلا بالله . والثاني أن عقد إيمان كل مؤمن أن لا الله فيما أمره ونهاه ؛ فمن عصاه لم يف به. يعصى ما كان اعتقاده موقوفا على ما يظهر بالابتلاء بقوله : ألم . أحسب الناس أن يتركوا مع أن يقولوا آمنا " الآية " ، وقال : وليعلمن المنافقين في موضع آخر. فثبت بذلك استحقاق اسم الكفر بما ظهر كذبه فيما أظهر من الاعتقاد والنظر يوجب ذلك، بما هو بالذي [ يفعل] مخالف فيه عن الله، مجيب للشيطان إلى ما دعاه ومطيع له فيما أمره؛ ومن ذلك وصفه فقد عبده، ومن عبد الشيطان فهو كافر . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Then some people established for him the name of kufr (disbelief) by two aspects: one of them is by His saying: 'And none shall enter it except the most wretched, who denies and turns away' [Al-Layl 92:15-16], and He said: 'And do We requite except the ingrate?' [Saba 34:17], and He said: 'And whoever does evil will be requited for it' [An-Nisa 4:123], and He said: 'And whoever comes with an evil deed will not be requited except its like', and He said: 'And whoever does an atom's weight of evil will see it' [Az-Zalzala 99:8]; so He established requital for what is small among it, and informed that He does not requite except the ingrate and none shall enter it except those mentioned, along with what Allah Almighty said: 'Indeed, those who harm Allah and His Messenger...' and every disobedient person harms the Messenger of Allah. And there is no power except with Allah. And the second is that the covenant of every believer's faith is that he does not disobey Allah in what He commanded and forbade him; so whoever disobeys Him has not fulfilled it. His belief was conditional upon what appears through trial by His saying: 'Alif Lam Meem. Do the people think that they will be left to say, "We believe"...' the verse, and He said: 'And He will surely make known the hypocrites' in another place. So it is thereby established that the name of kufr is deserved because his falsehood appeared in what he manifested of belief and consideration necessitating that, because he is contrary to Allah in what he does, answering the devil to what he called him and obedient to him in what he commanded him; and because of that he has described him, so he has worshipped him, and whoever worships the devil is a kafir. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 414

Commentary
This passage presents the arguments of a group of scholars who hold that a person committing a major sin deserves the name kafir (unbeliever). The author (Maturidi) is reporting this view, not necessarily endorsing it. The group provides two proofs from the Qur'an. First proof: They cite verses stating that only the 'most wretched' who deny and turn away enter Hell, and that requital is only for the ungrateful, and that every evil deed is requited. They argue that since every sinner is punished, and punishment is specified for the ungrateful, the sinner is therefore an ingrate (kafur). Additionally, harming Allah and His Messenger is associated with curse, and every disobedient person harms the Messenger. Second proof: The covenant of faith (mithaq) requires that a believer not disobey Allah in any command or prohibition. Disobedience thus breaks the covenant, and the person's faith is shown to be false through trials, as mentioned in the verses about hypocrisy. Therefore, the sinner deserves the name kafir because his actions reveal his true disbelief; he follows the devil and worships him, and whoever worships the devil is a kafir.

كافر
عقد الإيمان
ابتلاء
عصيان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Maturidi himself held that a major sinner is a kafir.
Correction: Maturidi is reporting the view of a group (قوم) and will later refute it. The passage should not be taken as his own position.

Checkpoint

B) Because the Qur'an states that only the ungrateful are punished
C) Because he harms the Messenger by disobeying
D) Both B and C

Key Takeaway
The arguments that a sinner is a kafir rely on linking punishment to ingratitude and viewing sin as a breach of the faith covenant that reveals hidden disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
If obeying the devil in a single act is equivalent to worshipping him, what does that say about the hidden loyalties in your heart? Are there areas where you are serving your own desires more than Allah?

The View That the Sinner Is a Mushrik, Not a Kafir
Maturidi reports the opinion that the sinner is a mushrik due to action-based shirk.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the reasoning of scholars who classify the sinner as a mushrik (polytheist) rather than a kafir, based on the concept of shirk in action derived from the Qur'an.

Core Idea
Sin by action can be classified as shirk, not kufr.

Maturidi reports a group of scholars who argue that a sinner should be called a mushrik, not a kafir. Their reasoning hinges on the distinction between sin committed by action versus speech, and they support this with Qur'anic verses that associate shirk with actions in worship. This lesson explores their evidence and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation carefully. Notice the key terms: shirk, mushrik, kufr, fi‘l, qawl.
Pay attention to the Qur'anic citations.
2
Identify the core argument
The group argues that sin is by action, not speech, and that the Qur'an mentions shirk in action. Find the specific verses they cite.
3
Reflect on the distinction
Consider how this view differs from calling the sinner a kafir. The commentary highlights that this shirk in action is minor, not major shirk.
This helps understand the nuance in early theological debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بـــ ومنهم من يسميه مشركاً لا كافرًا ، [لأنه ] إنها صار إلى ما صار بالفعل لا بالقول، وقد قال الله تعالى: فمن كان يرجو لقاء ربه فليعمل عملاً صالحاً ولا يشرك بعبادة ربه أحدا [الكهف، ۱۱۰/۱۸]، فجعل في العمل شركاً ، / وكذا تسمية أهل الشرك بما أشركوا في العبادة [١٧] غير الله ؛ وذلك معنى قوله : وما يؤمن أكثرهم بالله إلا وهم مشركون ﴾ [يوسف، ١٠٦/١٢]، وقال : إن الله لا يغفر أن يشرك به ﴾ [النساء، ٤ / ٤٨ ، ١١٦] الآية، وقد بينا أن ما يُغفر من الذنوب هي التي كانت على الخطأ أو الإكراه كما جاء به الكتاب . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And among them are those who call him a mushrik (polytheist), not a kafir, because he came to what he came to by deed, not by speech. And Allah Almighty said: 'So whoever hopes for the meeting with his Lord, let him do righteous work and not associate anyone in the worship of his Lord' [Al-Kahf 18:110], so He made shirk in action. Likewise, the naming of the people of shirk is due to what they associated in worship of other than Allah. And that is the meaning of His saying: 'And most of them believe not in Allah except while they associate others with Him' [Yusuf 12:106]. And He said: 'Indeed, Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him' [An-Nisa 4:48, 116] the verse. And we have explained that what is forgiven of sins are those that were due to error or coercion, as the Book has come with. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage presents a second group of scholars who hold a different view: the sinner should be called a mushrik (polytheist) rather than a kafir. Their reasoning is that the sin is committed by action (fi‘l) not by speech (qawl), and shirk can occur in action. They support this by citing the Qur'anic verse: 'So whoever hopes for the meeting with his Lord, let him do righteous work and not associate anyone in the worship of his Lord' (Al-Kahf 18:110), which prohibits shirk in worship through action. They argue that the polytheists (ahl al-shirk) are named so because of their actions in worshipping other than Allah. The verse 'And most of them believe not in Allah except while they associate others with Him' (Yusuf 12:106) indicates that belief can coexist with shirk in action. They also invoke the verse 'Allah does not forgive that partners be associated with Him,' implying that shirk is the unforgivable sin, but minor shirk (in action) may be forgiven if done in error or under coercion, as the author notes he has previously explained. Thus, the sinner is a mushrik, not a kafir, because his sin is an act of shirk in worship.

Key Terms
شرك
مشرك
كفر
فعل
قول
عبادة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Calling the sinner a mushrik means they are committing major shirk (worshipping other gods).
Correction: This view considers the sin as a form of shirk in action, which is minor and can be forgiven if done in error or coercion, not the major unforgivable shirk.

Checkpoint
Why does this group call the sinner a mushrik rather than a kafir?
A) Because the sin is committed by speech
B) Because the sin is committed by action, which can be shirk
C) Because they believe all sins are unforgivable
D) Because the sinner denies Allah

Key Takeaway
Sin committed by action can be considered shirk, but this is a minor shirk that does not remove one from faith entirely.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin by action—like lying, gossiping, or harming someone—do you ever consider that you are committing a form of shirk, prioritizing your desire over worship of Allah alone? How does that realization change the way you view your own sins and your need for repentance? Today, identify one action you habitually do that might be a form of action-based shirk and resolve to avoid it.
?
If even minor sins can be classified as shirk in action, what does that say about the seriousness with which you should approach every deed? Are there actions in your daily life that you do without thinking about Allah's commands? How can you turn those actions into acts of worship by correcting your intention? Commit to a specific change in one routine action starting now.
?
The passage mentions that sins done in error or under coercion are forgiven. But what about sins done willfully and repeatedly? Is there a sin you are struggling with that you keep excusing as 'just an action'? How can you break the cycle and turn back to Allah with sincere repentance, knowing that even action-based shirk is serious?

Maturidi on Minor Sins and Faith Preservation
Maturidi classifies sins and defends minor sinners' faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's classification of sins into minor and major, and his argument that committing minor sins does not remove a person from faith.

Core Idea
Minor sins do not break faith; Allah's promise of reward protects the believer.

In this passage, Maturidi presents a third group's division of sins into minor and major sins. He endorses their view on minor sins and defends the position that a person who commits minor sins remains a believer. The key argument is that subjecting such a believer to eternal punishment would contradict Allah's promise to reward even an atom's weight of good. The passage also begins to list reasons why the term 'kafir' or 'mushrik' should not apply to a minor sinner.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification of sins
The passage first presents that some scholars divided sins into two types: minor sins (صغائر) and major sins (كبائر). Note the conditions for forgiveness of minor sins: avoiding major sins, pardon, and requital.
2
Focus on the position on minor sins
Maturidi states that the view on minor sins—which he affirms as his own—is that committing them does not expel a person from faith. This is a key theological point: the sinner remains a believer.
This distinguishes from Kharijite and Mu'tazilite views.
3
Understand the argument against eternal punishment
Eternal punishment (الخلود في النار) for a believer who commits minor sins is invalid because it would necessitate a breach of Allah's promise. The verse 'So whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it' (99:7) guarantees reward for good deeds, so eternal punishment would contradict this promise.
4
Examine the first reason given
The passage begins to list reasons why the name 'kufr' or 'shirk' does not apply to such a sinner. The first reason is that Allah commanded His Prophet to seek forgiveness for believers, implying they remain believers despite their sins. The passage ends mid-sentence, but this reason is clear.
The remainder of the argument continues on the next page.

Then what prevents the name of kufr in reality and shirk are aspects, one of them is Allah's command to His Prophet to seek forgiveness

Commentary
This passage presents a third group's classification of sins into two categories: minor sins (sagha'ir) and major sins (kaba'ir). The author (Maturidi) states that this group's view on minor sins is also his own view: the person who commits minor sins is not expelled from faith (iman). Eternal punishment (khulud fi al-nar) for a believer is invalid because it would contradict Allah's promise of reward for good deeds, even an atom's weight, and for righteous deeds done by a believer. The author then begins to list reasons why the name kafir or mushrik should not be applied to a sinner. The first reason mentioned is that Allah commanded His Prophet to seek forgiveness for believers (presumably for their sins), which implies they remain believers. The text cuts off before completing the list, but it is clear that the author is defending the position that minor sins do not remove one from faith, and thus the sinner should not be called a kafir or mushrik.

Sins: Minor and Major According to Maturidi
Maturidi classifies sins and rules on minor sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's classification of sins into minor and major and the ruling that minor sins do not nullify faith.

Core Idea
Minor sins do not expel a believer from iman.

This passage from Imam Maturidi addresses a fundamental theological question: do all sins remove a person from faith? Maturidi distinguishes between minor and major sins, clarifying that minor sins are forgiven through avoiding major sins, divine pardon, or requital, and importantly, they do not cause expulsion from iman. This lesson unpacks that distinction and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the two categories of sins mentioned and the conditional forgiveness for minor sins.
2
Identify the two categories
Maturidi divides sins into two types: minor (صغائر) and major (كبائر). Find these terms and see how he describes each.
Minor sins are forgiven under certain conditions; major sins are a topic of disagreement.
3
Understand the ruling on minor sins
Maturidi affirms his position: a person who commits minor sins is not expelled from iman, and eternal punishment is incompatible with true faith. Grasp why this matters for Islamic theology.
4
Review the common misunderstanding
The passage guards against the idea that any sin nullifies faith. Contrast Maturidi's teaching with that misconception.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنهم من قسم الذنوب قسمين، فجعل منها صغائر تغفر باجتناب الكبائر وبالعفو وبالجزاء، ونحو ذلك على اختلاف أقاويلهم؛ وكبائر اختلفوا فيها على القولين الأولين. فأما الصغائر فقولهم فيها ـ وهو قولنا ـ أن لا يجوز إخراج صاحبها من الإيمان. وفاسد مع الإيمان الخلود في النار
And among them are those who divided sins into two divisions, and made of them minor sins that are forgiven by avoiding the major sins and by pardon and by requital, and similar to that according to the difference of their statements; and major sins about which they differed according to the two earlier opinions. As for the minor sins, their statement concerning them — and it is our statement — is that it is not permissible to expel the one who commits them from iman. And [the idea of] eternal abiding in the Fire is invalid along with iman.
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage is a classification of sins and a statement of the theological ruling on minor sins. Maturidi presents an opinion that divides sins into two categories: minor sins (sagha'ir) and major sins (kabair). Minor sins are forgiven by avoiding major sins, by God's pardon, or by requital of good deeds. He then affirms that the correct position (his own) is that a person who commits minor sins is not expelled from iman (faith). Moreover, the notion of eternal punishment in the Fire (khulud fi al-nar) is invalid for a person who has iman. This guards against the view that any sin nullifies faith.

Key Terms
صغائر
كبائر
إيمان
خلود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Committing any sin, even a minor one, removes a person from the state of iman (faith) and leads to eternal punishment.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that minor sins do not expel a person from iman, and the notion of eternal fire is incompatible with genuine faith.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the ruling on a person who commits a minor sin?
A) They are expelled from iman.
B) They are not expelled from iman.
C) They become a kafir.
D) They must be punished in hell eternally.

Key Takeaway
Minor sins do not remove a believer from iman, and eternal punishment is incompatible with true faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat minor sins as trivial because you know they don't expel you from iman, or do they still burden your conscience? What does your answer reveal about your reverence for Allah's commands?
?
If eternal punishment is incompatible with iman, then your iman is your ultimate safeguard. Are you actively nurturing your iman, or are you relying on it as a mere label while allowing minor sins to accumulate?
?
The passage mentions three ways minor sins are forgiven: avoiding major sins, pardon, and requital. Honestly examine yourself: are you striving to avoid major sins, or are you presuming on Allah's pardon without changing your behavior?

Proof from Istighfar: Sin Does Not Remove Iman
Maturidi proves that not every sin removes iman via the Prophet's command to seek forgiveness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the Prophet's command to seek forgiveness to prove that not every sin removes iman, and why this refutes the Mu'tazilite position.

Core Idea
The Prophet's istighfar for believers proves they sin yet remain believers.

Maturidi begins a powerful argument by looking at a divine command: the Prophet is told to seek forgiveness for believers. But if every sin removed iman, how could the Prophet pray for sinners who are still called believers? This passage walks through the logical options to conclude that iman is not lost by every sin, directly challenging the Mu'tazilite view.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Pay attention to the logical flow and the key terms.
2
Follow the argument by elimination
Maturidi eliminates several possibilities: the command cannot be for affirming kufr or shirk, nor for those who have lost iman, nor for sinless or forgiven people, nor can the Prophet's prayer be unanswered.
Each elimination brings us closer to the conclusion.
3
Identify the conclusion and refutation
The conclusion is that the name of iman is not removed by every sin; some sins require repentance. This directly refutes the Mu'tazilite position that any unforgiven sin removes iman.
Notice how the argument ends with a clear refutation.
4
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this matters: it affirms that believers can commit major sins without leaving the fold of iman, provided they repent. This has profound implications for how we view sin and forgiveness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الذي يمنع اسم الكفر في الحقيقة والشرك أوجه أحدها أمر الله نبيه أن يستغفر له وللمؤمنين والمؤمنات. ثم لا يُحتمل الأمر به على إثبات كفر أو شرك بقوله: ما كان للنبي والذين آمنوا أن يستغفروا للمشركين، وبما أمره أن يستغفر للمؤمنين. ومحال أن يأمره بالاستغفار باسم الإيمان وهو عنهم زائل، لأنه يوجب الكذب. ثم قد حذره الله عن الاستغفار لأهل الشرك بما ذكرت، ولأهل النفاق بقوله: سيقول لك المخلفون من الأعراب الآية، وقوله: سواء عليهم أستغفرت لهم الآية. وأيضا نهيه إياه عن الصلاة. فثبت أن أولئك الذين أمره بالاستغفار هم أهل الإيمان في الحقيقة. ثم لا يُحتمل أن يؤمر بالاستغفار ولا ذنوب لهم أو كانت مغفورة لهم، لأن الاستغفار هو طلب المغفرة، وطلبها لمن قد غفر له كتمان نعمة الغفران، وذلك كفران النعمة، بل حق ذلك الشكر والحمد. وما لا ذنب ثمة فيخرج طلب المغفرة مخرج كفران العصمة والسؤال أن لا يجور، إذ تعذيب مثله في حكمه جور. ثم لا يُحتمل أن يكون رسول الله والملائكة يستغفرون لمن أمروا به ثم لا يجابون. فثبت بهذا أن لا يزول اسم الإيمان بكل ذنب، وأن من الذنوب ما ليس بمغفور يغفر بالتوبة عنه؛ إذ ليس في استغفار غير المذنب توبة. وفي ذلك نقض على المعتزلة في إزالتهم اسم الإيمان بكل ذنب ليس بمغفور لصاحبه حتى يستغفر.
Then what prevents the name of kufr in reality and shirk are aspects, one of which is the command of God to His Prophet to seek forgiveness for himself and for the believing men and believing women. Then it is not plausible that the command is for affirming kufr or shirk by His saying: 'It is not for the Prophet and those who believe to seek forgiveness for the polytheists' [Quran 9:113], and by His commanding him to seek forgiveness for the believers. And it is impossible that He commands him to seek forgiveness in the name of iman while it [iman] is removed from them, because that would entail lying. Then God has warned him against seeking forgiveness for the people of shirk as I mentioned, and for the people of hypocrisy by His saying: 'The desert Arabs who stayed behind will say to you...' the verse, and His saying: 'It is the same for them whether you ask forgiveness for them or not...' the verse. And also His prohibition of him from praying [over them]. So it is established that those whom He commanded him to seek forgiveness for are the people of iman in reality. Then it is not plausible that he is commanded to seek forgiveness while they have no sins or their sins have been forgiven, because seeking forgiveness is the request of forgiveness, and requesting it for one who has been forgiven is concealing the blessing of forgiveness, and that is ingratitude for the blessing, rather the right of that is thankfulness and praise. And if there is no sin there, then the request of forgiveness comes out as ingratitude for protection [from sin] and the request that He not treat unjustly, since punishing someone like that would be injustice in His judgment. Then it is not plausible that the Messenger of God and the angels seek forgiveness for those whom they are commanded to [seek forgiveness for] and then they are not answered. So it is established by this that the name of iman is not removed by every sin, and that among sins are those that are not forgiven [except] that they are forgiven by repentance from them; for there is no repentance in the seeking of forgiveness by one who has not sinned. And in that is a refutation of the Mu'tazila in their removal of the name of iman for every sin that is not forgiven for its doer until he seeks forgiveness.
Source pages p. 415

Commentary
This passage is a proof (istidlal) drawn from the divine command to the Prophet to seek forgiveness for believers. Maturidi argues by elimination: the command cannot be to affirm kufr or shirk, because God forbade seeking forgiveness for polytheists. It also cannot be for those who have lost iman, because that would make God's command false. The Prophet was commanded to seek forgiveness specifically for believers, as established by verses warning against seeking forgiveness for polytheists and hypocrites. Therefore, those for whom istighfar is commanded are true believers. Next, Maturidi argues that if these believers had no sins or their sins were already forgiven, seeking forgiveness would be inappropriate—it would be concealing a blessing or implying that God might act unjustly. Hence, they must have sins that are not yet forgiven. Furthermore, it is inconceivable that the Prophet and angels would beg for forgiveness and not be answered. The conclusion is that iman is not removed by every sin; some sins require repentance (tawba) for forgiveness. This refutes the Mu'tazilite position that any sin not forgiven removes the name of iman permanently.

Key Terms
استغفار
إيمان
كفر
شرك
توبة
معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Prophet's istighfar for believers implies that they have lost iman because they are sinners.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the command to seek forgiveness actually confirms they are believers, as seeking forgiveness for non-believers is forbidden. Thus, they have iman despite their sins.

Checkpoint
What is the main conclusion Maturidi draws from the command to seek forgiveness for believers?
A) Believers never sin.
B) All sins remove iman.
C) Not every sin removes iman; some require repentance.
D) Seeking forgiveness is only for non-believers.

Key Takeaway
The Prophet's command to seek forgiveness for believers demonstrates that believers can sin without losing iman, refuting the Mu'tazilite claim that any unforgiven sin removes iman.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you truly believe that your iman remains intact, or do you sometimes feel that a particular sin has severed your connection with Allah? How does this lesson challenge or affirm your belief, and what will you do to strengthen your conviction in the mercy of Allah that keeps iman despite sin?
?
If the Prophet himself was commanded to seek forgiveness for believers, does that make you complacent about your sins, thinking 'the Prophet will pray for me so I don't need to repent'? How does Maturidi's insistence that forgiveness requires tawba (repentance) compel you to examine your own repentance habits and make a specific change today?
?
Are you guilty of believing that some of your sins are too great to be forgiven, or that you are not a 'real' believer because of your faults? This lesson affirms that believers sin but remain believers. Which sin have you been too ashamed to repent from, and what step will you take this week to turn back to Allah sincerely?

Proof that Faith Remains Despite Sin
Maturidi argues that seeking forgiveness for believers implies they have sins, so faith remains.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the command to seek forgiveness for believers proves that faith is not removed by sin, and how this refutes the Mu'tazila and Khawarij.

Core Idea
Faith remains despite sin; seeking forgiveness proves believers have sins but keep their faith.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents a powerful argument from the Quran and reason to show that committing a sin does not automatically expel a person from faith. He uses the fact that the Prophet was commanded to seek forgiveness for believers—an act that only makes sense if they have sins yet remain believers. This lesson will walk you through his reasoning and show how it challenges the views of the Mu'tazila and Khawarij.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic text and its literal translation. Pay attention to the key Quranic references and how the argument unfolds.
Notice how Maturidi uses Quran 9:113 and other verses to establish his point.
2
Identify the main proof
The core proof is that the Prophet was commanded to seek forgiveness (istighfar) for believers. This command would be impossible if the believers had no sins or if their faith was removed. Trace how Maturidi deduces that they must have sins but still be believers.
The word 'istighfar' means to request forgiveness, which presupposes sin.
3
Understand the logical refutation of the Mu'tazila and Khawarij
Maturidi concludes that the name of faith (iman) does not leave a person due to any sin. He specifically refutes the Mu'tazila who say a sinner remains in a middle state without faith, and the Khawarij who say a sinner becomes a disbeliever.
Notice how the passage ends with a clear rejection of both sectarian positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
له وللمؤمنين والمؤمنات . ثم لا يُحتمل الأمر به على إثبات كفر أو شرك بقوله : ما كان للنبي والذين آمنوا أن يستغفروا للمشركين وبما أمره أن يستغفر للمؤمنين. ومحال أن يأمره بالاستغفار باسم الإيمان وهو عنهم زائل، لأنه يوجب الكذب. ثم قد حذره الله عن الاستغفار لأهل الشرك بما ذكرت، ولأهل النفاق بقوله : وسيقول لك المخلفون من الأعراب الآية، وقوله : سواء عليهم أستغفرت لهم الآية. وأيضا نهيه إياه عن الصلاة. فثبت أن أولئك الذين أمره بالاستغفار هم أهل الإيمان في الحقيقة. ثم لا يُحتمل أن يؤمر بالاستغفار ولا ذنوب لهم أو كانت مغفورة لهم، لأن الاستغفار هو طلب المغفرة، وطلبها لمن قد غفر له كتمان نعمة الغفران، وذلك كفران النعمة، بل حق ذلك الشكر والحمد. وما لا ذنب ثمة فيخرج طلب المغفرة مخرج كفران العصمة والسؤال أن لا يجور، إذ تعذيب مثله في حكمه جور. ثم لا يُحتمل أن يكون رسول الله والملائكة يستغفرون لمن أمروا به ثم لا يجابون. فثبت بهذا أن لا يزول اسم الإيمان بكل ذنب، وأن من الذنوب ما ليس بمغفور يغفر بالتوبة عنه؛ إذ ليس في استغفار غير المذنب توبة. وفي ذلك نقض على المعتزلة في إزالتهم اسم الإيمان بكل ذنب ليس بمغفور لصاحبه حتى يستغفر، ونقض على الخوارج بما ذكرنا. والله اعلم.
For him and for the believing men and believing women. Then it is not plausible that the command concerning it entails affirming disbelief or shirk by His saying: 'It is not for the Prophet and those who believe to seek forgiveness for the polytheists' [Quran 9:113], and by His commanding him to seek forgiveness for the believers. And it is impossible that He command him to seek forgiveness in the name of faith while it is removed from them, because that would necessitate lying. Then He warned him against seeking forgiveness for the people of shirk as I mentioned, and for the people of hypocrisy by His saying: 'Those who stayed behind among the Bedouins will say to you...' the verse, and His saying: 'It is the same to them whether you seek forgiveness for them or not...' the verse. And also His prohibition of prayer for them. So it is established that those whom He commanded him to seek forgiveness for are indeed the people of faith in reality. Then it is not plausible that he be commanded to seek forgiveness while they have no sins or while their sins are already forgiven, because seeking forgiveness is the request for forgiveness, and requesting it for one who has already been forgiven is concealing the blessing of forgiveness, and that is ingratitude for the blessing; rather, the due of that is gratitude and praise. And if there is no sin there, then the request for forgiveness takes the form of ingratitude for infallibility and a request that He should not act unjustly, since punishing someone like that would be unjust in His judgment. Then it is not plausible that the Messenger of Allah and the angels seek forgiveness for those they are commanded to seek forgiveness for and then not be answered. So it is established by this that the name of faith is not removed by every sin, and that among sins there are those that are not forgiven except by repentance; because the seeking forgiveness of one who is not sinning does not constitute repentance. And in that is a refutation against the Mu'tazila in their removal of the name of faith for every sin that is not forgiven for its doer until he seeks forgiveness, and a refutation against the Khawarij by what we have mentioned. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 416

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the name of faith (iman) is not removed by sin, refuting the Mu'tazila and Khawarij. The author begins by noting that the Prophet was commanded to seek forgiveness for the believers. If the command implied that they were disbelievers or polytheists, it would contradict the Quranic prohibition of seeking forgiveness for polytheists. Therefore, those for whom the Prophet was commanded to seek forgiveness are true believers. Then he argues that seeking forgiveness for them implies they have sins, otherwise the act would be ingratitude. Hence, believers commit sins but remain believers. He concludes that the name of faith remains despite sins, and repentance is necessary for forgiveness. This refutes Mu'tazila who remove faith for sins not forgiven without repentance, and Khawarij who label such sinners as disbelievers.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that any sin automatically removes the name of faith, making the sinner a disbeliever or in a middle state.
Correction: The passage proves that faith remains despite sin; the sinner is still a believer, but may need repentance for forgiveness.

Checkpoint

D) Because the name of faith requires sin to be removed.

Key Takeaway
The command to seek forgiveness for believers proves they have sins yet remain believers, so faith is not removed by sin—refuting the Mu'tazila and Khawarij.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that the name of faith is not removed by sin, does that make you more complacent about your own sins? How does this lesson's emphasis on repentance—rather than just the absence of sin—call you to actively turn back to Allah today, even for sins you have been ignoring?
?
Think of a fellow Muslim whose sin you are aware of. Have you ever secretly doubted their faith because of that sin? This passage commands you to view them as believers for whom the Prophet sought forgiveness. Will you change your attitude, pray for them sincerely, and treat them as brothers and sisters in faith?
?
The Prophet (peace be upon him) was commanded to seek forgiveness for believers, including you, despite your sins. Are you truly grateful for this mercy? What specific sin have you been neglecting to repent from? Will you set a time right now to make sincere tawba and begin anew?

The Command to Repent While Affirming Faith
Maturidi cites repentance verses and draws two refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses Quranic commands to repent, while addressing believers, to refute both the Mu'tazila and Khawarij positions on the status of sinful believers.

Core Idea
Believers retain the name of faith even when they sin and need repentance.

Imam Maturidi continues his refutation by citing verses that command believers to repent. These verses show that the title of faith remains even when repentance is required, challenging both the Mu'tazila who deny faith to grave sinners and the Khawarij who label them as disbelievers. Let's examine the verses and Maturidi's argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the translation. Notice the flow of the argument.
2
Identify the Quranic verses cited
Find the two verses about repentance that Maturidi quotes. Observe how they address the believers directly.
Verses: 24:31 and 66:8.
3
Understand the refutation of the Mu'tazila
Maturidi argues that the Mu'tazila are wrong to remove the name of faith for grave sinners, because the verses affirm faith while commanding repentance.
4
Understand the refutation of the Khawarij
Against the Khawarij, Maturidi points out that calling such sinners disbelievers contradicts the Quranic address 'O you who believe' and the command to repent.
The Khawarij label them as disbelievers, which is impossible with the name of faith.
5
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that the verses obligate repentance while affirming faith, proving that sinners remain believers.
This reinforces the Sunni position that faith is not lost by sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن الله تعالى قال في الذنوب التي لا يغفرها سواء عليهم أستغفرت الآية، وعلى ذلك قال: وتوبوا إلى الله جميعا أيها المؤمنون لعلكم تفلحون، وقال: يا أيها الذين آمنوا توبوا إلى الله توبة نصوحا الآية، فألزمهم التوبة مع إثبات الإيمان، وأخبر أنه بالتوبة يغفر لهم. وفي ذلك وجهان أحدهما على المعتزلة في إزالتهم اسم الإيمان في كل ذنب لا يُغفر عندهم إلا بالتوبة، وفي ذلك إثباته، وعلى الخوارج بتسميتهم كفرة وأهل الشرك، ومحال مع ذلك اسم الإيمان والأمر بغيره. والله الموفق. ولو كان في شيء تسمية بالكفر فهو على مجاز اللغة من حيث ذلك صنيعهم ونحو ذلك؛ على ما يقال للمرء أصم وأعمى بما لا يقف على حقيقة ما بذلك يوصل إليه. وذلك نحو قوله: ومن كفر بالله من بعد إيمانه الآية، فأثبت اسم الكفر فيما كان منه على الإكراه لفظا لا تحقيقا لما اطمأن قلبه بالإيمان فثبت أن قد يجوز تسميته لنوازل مجازا، فمثله الأعمال. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And also, Allah Almighty said concerning sins that He does not forgive: 'It is the same to them whether you seek forgiveness for them or not' the verse, and upon that He said: 'And repent to Allah all together, O believers, that you may succeed' [Quran 24:31], and He said: 'O you who believe, repent to Allah with sincere repentance' the verse. So He obligated repentance upon them while affirming faith, and informed that through repentance He forgives them. And in that are two aspects: one against the Mu'tazila in their removal of the name of faith for every sin that is not forgiven in their view except by repentance, while that (verse) affirms it; and against the Khawarij in their labeling them as disbelievers and people of shirk, while it is impossible with that (label) to have the name of faith and the command to something other than it. And Allah is the Giver of success. And if there is any labeling of disbelief, it is metaphorically in the language, in terms of their deeds and the like; as one says of a person that he is deaf and blind when he does not perceive the reality of what is thereby reached. And that is like His saying: 'Whoever disbelieves in Allah after his faith...' the verse, so He affirmed the name of disbelief in what was from him under coercion, verbally not actually, because his heart is at rest with faith. So it is established that it may be permissible to label him for occurrences metaphorically, and likewise deeds. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation by citing the Quranic command to repent while addressing the believers as 'O you who believe.' This shows that the title of faith remains even when sins require repentance. The author points out two refutations: against Mu'tazila who say that such sinners are not believers until they repent, and against Khawarij who call them disbelievers. Then he discusses metaphorical labeling: sometimes one is called a disbeliever figuratively based on deeds, as in the case of coercion where the utterance of disbelief is verbal but the heart is firm in faith. So metaphorical usage does not imply true disbelief.

Key Terms
توبة
اسم الإيمان
معتزلة
خوارج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that committing a major sin removes one from the fold of faith until repentance.
Correction: The Quran addresses sinners as 'believers' and commands them to repent, proving that they retain the name of faith.

Checkpoint
Why does the command 'O you who believe, repent to Allah' refute the Mu'tazila?
A) Because it shows that repentance is only for believers.
B) Because it affirms the name of faith for those who are commanded to repent.
C) Because it proves that all believers are sinless.
D) Because it commands repentance, which the Mu'tazila reject.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic command to repent while addressing believers proves that sinners retain the name of faith, refuting both Mu'tazila and Khawarij.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you sin, do you still consider yourself a believer, or do you feel you have lost the name of faith until you repent? How does this passage challenge your feeling?
?
The Quran commands believers to repent. Are you living as a believer who repents regularly, or do you neglect repentance because you think it's only for disbelievers?
?
Both Mu'tazila and Khawarij errored by either downplaying or overplaying the effect of sin on faith. Which direction do you tend towards in your own thinking: being too harsh on yourself or too lenient?

Metaphorical Labeling of Disbelief
Maturidi discusses metaphorical disbelief using the coercion verse and an analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi explains that attributions of disbelief in the Quran can be metaphorical, as seen in the case of coercion and deeds.

Core Idea
Disbelief can be attributed metaphorically based on apparent deeds, not actual loss of faith.

In the second part of this passage, Maturidi addresses metaphorical language. He explains that when the Quran labels someone a 'disbeliever' under coercion, it is metaphorical, as the heart remains faithful. Similarly, deeds can be called disbelief metaphorically. This clarifies that not every Quranic use of 'kufr' implies actual apostasy.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on metaphorical disbelief
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice how Maturidi introduces the idea of metaphorical language starting with 'ولو كان'.
2
Understand the analogy of deaf/blind
Maturidi gives an analogy: calling a person deaf or blind metaphorically when they do not perceive something. Similarly, 'kufr' can be metaphorical.
This establishes the principle of metaphorical usage.
3
Analyze the coercion verse
Maturidi cites the verse about coerced disbelief (Quran 16:106) to show that the label 'disbeliever' is applied verbally but not actually, because the heart is at peace with faith.
4
Extend to deeds
Maturidi concludes that similarly, deeds can be called disbelief metaphorically.
This refutes those who take such literal labels as evidence of real disbelief.
5
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this argument protects the status of believers who commit acts that resemble disbelief under duress.
This is important for understanding apparent contradictions in the Quran.

Source Text

And Allah is the Giver of success. And if there is any labeling of disbelief, it is metaphorically in the language, in terms of their deeds and the like; as one says of a person that he is deaf and blind when he does not perceive the reality of what is thereby reached. And that is like His saying: 'Whoever disbelieves in Allah after his faith...' the verse, so He affirmed the name of disbelief in what was from him under coercion, verbally not actually, because his heart is at rest with faith. So it is established that it may be permissible to label him for occurrences metaphorically, and likewise deeds. And there is no power except with Allah.

Commentary
This passage continues the refutation by citing the Quranic command to repent while addressing the believers as 'O you who believe.' This shows that the title of faith remains even when sins require repentance.
