Proof that good deeds are only rewarded with faith
Maturidi uses the atom's weight verse to show deeds are only rewarded after faith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi uses the Qur'anic verse about the atom's weight to prove that good deeds are only rewarded in the context of faith, not shirk.

Core Idea
Good deeds are only seen—rewarded—after faith, not with shirk.

Maturidi presents a concise proof from the Qur'an to show that good deeds are not rewarded when mixed with shirk. This passage clarifies that the promise of reward applies only in the state of faith, not disbelief. Let's examine the verse and its implication.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the English translation. Let the words of Imam Maturidi come through before interpreting.
2
Identify key terms
Find the terms مثقال ذرة (atom's weight), خير (good), شرك (shirk), إيمان (faith). Notice how they relate to the argument.
These terms are central to understanding the proof.
3
Understand the logical flow
The verse says everyone will see their good deeds. But Maturidi adds that this seeing (reward) does not happen with shirk or disbelief; it happens only after faith. Consider the implication: reward requires faith.
4
Reflect on the doctrinal point
This passage supports that faith is necessary for reward, and that sins do not negate faith. The name of faith remains even if one commits sins, as the reward is seen after entering faith.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you have grasped the main idea from this passage alone.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن الله تعالى قال: فمن يعمل مثقال ذرة خيرا يره الآية، ثم معلوم أنه لا يرى الخير وجزاءه مع الشرك، ولا جزاء شر في حال الكفر فيرى ذلك بعد الإيمان.
And also, Allah Almighty said: 'So whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it' the verse. Then it is known that he does not see the good and its reward with shirk, nor any reward for evil in the state of disbelief; so he sees that only after faith.
Source pages p. 417

Commentary
This passage presents another proof using the verse about seeing good deeds. Good deeds are only seen (rewarded) in the context of faith, not under shirk. Similarly, punishment for evil deeds is also seen only after faith. This supports that faith is necessary for reward, and that the name of faith remains even if one commits sins.

Key Terms
مثقال ذرة
خير
شرك
إيمان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Good deeds are rewarded regardless of one's faith status.
Correction: Maturidi shows that reward of good deeds only occurs after faith, not with shirk or disbelief.

Checkpoint
What does the verse 'Whoever does an atom's weight of good will see it' imply when combined with faith?
A) That good deeds are only rewarded under faith, not under shirk.
B) That good deeds are rewarded even without faith.
C) That evil deeds are also rewarded.
D) That faith is not necessary for reward.

Key Takeaway
Good deeds are only rewarded when accompanied by faith; shirk nullifies the reward.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a good deed you are particularly proud of. Now ask yourself: Was that deed accompanied by any form of shirk—even subtle, like showing off? According to Maturidi, if shirk was present, that deed will not be rewarded. What are you going to do about that? Will you repent and renew your faith today?
?
When you do something wrong, do you assume God will punish you only after you have faith? This lesson says punishment for evil is also seen only after faith. Does that make you more aware of your accountability? How will you change your actions starting today?

Contradiction with Divine Attributes
Maturidi shows how the Khawarij view negates divine mercy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Khawarij view on minor sins contradicts God's attributes of pardon, forgiveness, and mercy.

Core Idea
Calling minor sins kufr negates God's names like Pardoner and Forgiving.

Maturidi begins his refutation by showing that the Khawarij position on minor sins directly contradicts the very nature of God as revealed in the Qur'an. If no slip can be forgiven without repentance, then terms like 'Pardoner' and 'Forgiver' lose their meaning. This lesson examines this contradiction and its logical consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to grasp Maturidi's concise argument.
Notice the strong language: 'abandoned statement' and 'greatest of sins.'
2
Understand the core contradiction
Identify how calling minor sins kufr undermines God's attributes of forgiveness and mercy.
Maturidi argues that if no sin can be forgiven without repentance, God's names become meaningless.
3
Analyze the second consequence
Examine how this view leads to ascribing harshness to God and hostility from His servants.
This consequence shows the practical danger of such a belief.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القول في جعل الصغائر كفرًا أو شركا، أو التخليد في النار جزاء لها قول مهجور بما يسقط معنى تسميته عفوا غفورا رحيما، إذ لا يسعه مأثم ولا زلة بلا توبة؛ ويوجب به بعد أن عرفه عفوا غفورا كريما، وعادي لأجله من أزال عنه هذا الاسم إلى كل ما يوصف به كل قاس وكل لئيم؛ وبه يستحق الذي قال، إذ هذا أعظم الذنوب حيث بدل صفات الرب. ثم بما بلى به الأنبياء، فيكفر بهم في تلك الأحوال؛ ومن كفر بنبي في وقت فهو كافر لا ريب فيه. ثم بهذا وصف الرب بالجور لما فيه إبطال الحسنات بزلة، والعدل هو الذي يجزي بالإحسان والإساءة فيما أظهر عز وجل من كرمه؛ ثم بالتجهيل بما لم يعرف من يصلح للرسالة ويقوم بأداء الأمانة. ثم بما لا أحد خال عنه، فيكون في الذي ذكر تكليف ما لا يطاق؛ ثم ينقطع منه الخوف والرجاء ويحصل الأمر على الأمن والإياس، وقد شهد عليهما بالضلال والكفر. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then the statement about making minor sins disbelief or shirk, or eternal punishment in the Fire as recompense for them, is an abandoned statement because it negates the meaning of naming Him 'Pardoner', 'Forgiver', 'Merciful', since no sin or slip can be within His capacity without repentance; and it necessitates that after He was known as Pardoner, Forgiving, Generous, one becomes hostile because of it to whoever removes this name from Him to every quality of every harsh and vile person; and by it the one who said it deserves [punishment], for this is the greatest of sins because it replaces the attributes of the Lord. Then what He afflicted the prophets with, thus they are disbelieved in those states; and whoever disbelieves in a prophet at any time is a disbeliever without doubt. Then by this one describes the Lord with injustice because it nullifies good deeds with a single slip, while justice is that which rewards for good and evil in what He, the Exalted, manifested of His generosity; then by ignorance of what is not known about who is fit for prophethood and can fulfill the trust. Then by what no one is free from, thus in that which is mentioned is an obligation beyond capacity; then fear and hope are cut off from him and the matter becomes security and despair, and He has testified against both [by calling them] misguidance and disbelief. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the absurd consequences that follow from the Khawarij position that minor sins are tantamount to disbelief or shirk, leading to eternal punishment. Maturidi lists several logical and theological results: First, it contradicts God's attributes of being 'Pardoner', 'Forgiver', 'Merciful', because if no sin can be forgiven without repentance, then these attributes become meaningless. Second, it leads to hostility against God by removing His attribute of forgiveness and ascribing harshness to Him. Third, it necessitates takfir of prophets who committed minor slips, since if minor sins are disbelief, then prophets become disbelievers, which is absurd. Fourth, it describes God as unjust, because it nullifies accumulated good deeds for a single slip, whereas true justice rewards both good and evil appropriately. Fifth, it implies ignorance on God's part, because He would choose messengers who are not fit if they are condemned for minor slips. Sixth, it imposes an obligation beyond capacity, since no one is free from minor sins. Seventh, it eliminates fear and hope, leading to either false security or despair, both of which are condemned as misguidance and disbelief in the Quran. Thus, the Khawarij position is self-refuting.

Key Terms
الصغائر
الكفر
الشرك
صفات الله
الخوارج

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that all sins, including minor ones, require repentance for forgiveness and that God cannot forgive otherwise.
Correction: Maturidi argues this negates God's names of Pardoner and Forgiving, which indicate His generous mercy.

Checkpoint
What is the first consequence Maturidi mentions of the Khawarij position?
A) It leads to declaring prophets disbelievers.
B) It negates the meaning of God's names 'Pardoner', 'Forgiver', 'Merciful'.
C) It imposes obligations beyond capacity.
D) It eliminates fear and hope.

Key Takeaway
The Khawarij position on minor sins is self-refuting because it contradicts God's core attributes of mercy and forgiveness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever dismiss your own minor sins, thinking they are too small to matter? How does this passage's warning about attributing harshness to God confront your casual attitude, and what specific change will you make today in how you view and repent from small missteps?
?
If you have ever judged another Muslim harshly for a small mistake, how does Maturidi's argument about God's attributes of pardon and mercy expose that harshness as a form of misrepresenting God? What concrete step will you take to replace that judgment with mercy?
?
God is called al-‘Afuww (the Pardoner) even before you repent for a minor sin. How does that truth change your hope in God's mercy, and how will you live today in light of that hope without falling into presumption?

Further Absurdities: Prophets, Justice, and Hope
Maturidi lists consequences involving prophets, justice, and psychology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Khawarij view leads to absurdities such as declaring prophets disbelievers, describing God as unjust, and eliminating fear and hope.

Core Idea
Calling minor sins kufr leads to takfir of prophets and denies God's justice.

Maturidi continues his refutation by exposing more contradictions of the Khawarij position. If minor sins are kufr, then prophets who committed minor slips would be disbelievers, which is impossible. Furthermore, this view makes God unjust by nullifying good deeds with a single slip, and it destroys the balance of fear and hope in faith. This lesson explores these further consequences.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read the Arabic and translation from 'Then what He afflicted the prophets' onwards.
Notice the logical progression: each consequence builds on the previous one.
2
Trace the argument on prophets
Understand why the Khawarij view necessitates takfir of prophets.
Prophets are innocent of major sins but may commit minor slips (zalla). If minor sins are kufr, they become kafir.
3
Reflect on justice and human capacity
Consider how the view makes God unjust and imposes impossible obligations.
Maturidi says it nullifies good deeds and removes fear and hope.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم القول في جعل الصغائر كفرًا أو شركا، أو التخليد في النار جزاء لها قول مهجور بما يسقط معنى تسميته عفوا غفورا رحيما، إذ لا يسعه مأثم ولا زلة بلا توبة؛ ويوجب به بعد أن عرفه عفوا غفورا كريما، وعادي لأجله من أزال عنه هذا الاسم إلى كل ما يوصف به كل قاس وكل لئيم؛ وبه يستحق الذي قال، إذ هذا أعظم الذنوب حيث بدل صفات الرب. ثم بما بلى به الأنبياء، فيكفر بهم في تلك الأحوال؛ ومن كفر بنبي في وقت فهو كافر لا ريب فيه. ثم بهذا وصف الرب بالجور لما فيه إبطال الحسنات بزلة، والعدل هو الذي يجزي بالإحسان والإساءة فيما أظهر عز وجل من كرمه؛ ثم بالتجهيل بما لم يعرف من يصلح للرسالة ويقوم بأداء الأمانة. ثم بما لا أحد خال عنه، فيكون في الذي ذكر تكليف ما لا يطاق؛ ثم ينقطع منه الخوف والرجاء ويحصل الأمر على الأمن والإياس، وقد شهد عليهما بالضلال والكفر. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then the statement about making minor sins disbelief or shirk, or eternal punishment in the Fire as recompense for them, is an abandoned statement because it negates the meaning of naming Him 'Pardoner', 'Forgiver', 'Merciful', since no sin or slip can be within His capacity without repentance; and it necessitates that after He was known as Pardoner, Forgiving, Generous, one becomes hostile because of it to whoever removes this name from Him to every quality of every harsh and vile person; and by it the one who said it deserves [punishment], for this is the greatest of sins because it replaces the attributes of the Lord. Then what He afflicted the prophets with, thus they are disbelieved in those states; and whoever disbelieves in a prophet at any time is a disbeliever without doubt. Then by this one describes the Lord with injustice because it nullifies good deeds with a single slip, while justice is that which rewards for good and evil in what He, the Exalted, manifested of His generosity; then by ignorance of what is not known about who is fit for prophethood and can fulfill the trust. Then by what no one is free from, thus in that which is mentioned is an obligation beyond capacity; then fear and hope are cut off from him and the matter becomes security and despair, and He has testified against both [by calling them] misguidance and disbelief. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 419

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration of the absurd consequences that follow from the Khawarij position that minor sins are tantamount to disbelief or shirk, leading to eternal punishment. Maturidi lists several logical and theological results: First, it contradicts God's attributes of being 'Pardoner', 'Forgiver', 'Merciful', because if no sin can be forgiven without repentance, then these attributes become meaningless. Second, it leads to hostility against God by removing His attribute of forgiveness and ascribing harshness to Him. Third, it necessitates takfir of prophets who committed minor slips, since if minor sins are disbelief, then prophets become disbelievers, which is absurd. Fourth, it describes God as unjust, because it nullifies accumulated good deeds for a single slip, whereas true justice rewards both good and evil appropriately. Fifth, it implies ignorance on God's part, because He would choose messengers who are not fit if they are condemned for minor slips. Sixth, it imposes an obligation beyond capacity, since no one is free from minor sins. Seventh, it eliminates fear and hope, leading to either false security or despair, both of which are condemned as misguidance and disbelief in the Quran. Thus, the Khawarij position is self-refuting.

Key Terms
الأنبياء
التكفير
العدل
الجور
الخوف
الرجاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Believing that any sin, even minor, automatically makes a person a disbeliever and deserving of eternal punishment.
Correction: Maturidi shows this leads to takfir of prophets and contradicts God's mercy and justice.

Checkpoint

B) Prophets would be declared disbelievers due to minor slips.
C) Prophets would be rewarded for minor sins.
D) Prophets would be exempt from punishment.

The Khawarij position leads to theological absurdities that contradict the nature of God, the status of prophets, and the balance of fear and hope in faith.

Maturidi's Proof from Verses for Major Sinners as Disbelievers
Maturidi uses Qur'anic verses to argue that major sinners incur the label kafir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Maturidi uses Qur'anic verses and a naming argument to establish that a perpetrator of a major sin incurs the label of disbelief.

Core Idea
Qur'anic threats and names for disbelievers apply to major sinners.

In this passage, Imam al-Maturidi presents a multi-part argument that a person who commits a major sin should be considered a disbeliever. He starts with the logic of threat (wa'id), then cites verses that link despair of Allah's mercy with disbelief, and finally draws a comparison between the names applied to major sinners and those applied to disbelievers.

Guided Path
1
Understand the differentiation logic
Notice how Maturidi uses the distinction between minor and major sins: minor sins may be pardoned and the name of faith retained, so the threat must apply to major sins. This sets up the premise.
2
Examine the despair verses
Read the verses from Surah Yusuf (12:87) and Surah al-Hijr (15:56) that associate despair of Allah's mercy with disbelief and being astray. Reflect on how they support the argument.
3
Analyze the judgment verse
The verse from al-Ma'idah (5:44) declares that those who do not judge by Allah's law are disbelievers. Maturidi links the major sinner to this description.
The Arabic phrase here may require careful reading due to textual ambiguity.
4
Reflect on the naming argument
Maturidi concludes by noting that major sinners are already called by names used for disbelievers (fasiq, fajir, zalim). Therefore, consistency demands that they also be called disbelievers.
This is a linguistic and logical extension.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فتفريق من ذكرت بين الصغائر والكبائر فيما يثبت في الصغائر من إمكان العفو أو إبقاء اسم الإيمان أوجب صرف الوعيد إلى الكبائر؛ وما يثبت من ذكر جزاء الكفر والشرك ونحوه يوجب تحقيق اسم الشرك على قول قوم، والكفر على قول. وأيّد ذلك قوله تعالى: «إنه لا ييأس من روح الله إلا القوم الكافرون» [يوسف: 87]، وقال: «ومن يقنط من رحمة ربه إلا الضالون» [الحجر: 56]. ما كان صاحب الكبيرة حاكما بغير الذي أنزل الله، مع وتاركا الحكم به، وقد قال الله تعالى: «ومن لم يحكم بما أنزل الله فأولئك هم الكافرون» [المائدة: 44]. وبعد، فإنه قد سُمّي بالأسماء التي سمى الله بها الكفرة من الفسق والفجور والظلم لزمه أيضاً اسم الكفر.
So the differentiation by those mentioned between minor and major sins regarding what is established for minor sins—the possibility of pardon or retention of the name of faith—necessitated directing the threat to major sins; and what is established of mentioning the recompense of disbelief and idolatry and the like necessitates realizing the name of idolatry according to some people, and disbelief according to others. And this is supported by His saying: 'Indeed, no one despairs of relief from Allah except the disbelieving people' [Yusuf 12:87], and He said: 'And who despairs of the mercy of his Lord except the astray?' [Al-Hijr 15:56]. The perpetrator of a major sin was one who judges by other than what Allah has revealed, while abandoning judgement by it, and Allah said: 'And whoever does not judge by what Allah has revealed – then it is those who are the disbelievers' [Al-Ma'idah 5:44]. Moreover, since he has been named with the names by which Allah named the disbelievers—transgression, immorality, and injustice—then the name of disbelief also becomes necessary for him.
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage presents a proof that the perpetrator of a major sin should be considered a disbeliever (kafir). Maturidi argues that those who differentiate between minor and major sins acknowledge that minor sins may be pardoned and the name of faith retained, so threats of punishment must apply to major sins. He then cites two Qur'anic verses that associate despair of Allah's mercy with disbelief. Additionally, he links the major sinner to the verse about judging by other than Allah's law, which declares such people disbelievers. Finally, he uses a comparative argument: since the major sinner is called by names used for disbelievers (fasiq, fajir, zalim), the name kafir must also apply, because the same logic that applies those names applies the name of disbelief.

Key Terms

فجور
ظلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think Maturidi is claiming that every major sinner is automatically and irrevocably a disbeliever in all senses.
Correction: Maturidi's argument is based on the application of Qur'anic names and threats; it does not necessarily mean the sinner has no faith at all in the heart, but rather that the label 'kafir' is warranted based on the revealed texts.

Key Takeaway
Imam al-Maturidi uses Qur'anic verses and linguistic consistency to argue that the perpetrator of a major sin should be called a disbeliever.

The Two Divisions: Believer and Disbeliever
Maturidi argues from divine division of people and answers the objection about names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the major sinner must be a disbeliever based on the Quranic division of people into only two categories, and his response to the objection that names carry no inherent significance.

Core Idea
The Quran divides humanity into two groups: believers and disbelievers, leaving no third option for major sinners.

In this passage, Maturidi continues his refutation of those who claim the major sinner is neither a believer nor a disbeliever. He builds his case from Qur'anic verses that split people into exactly two categories, with no middle ground. He then anticipates an objection: that the name 'disbeliever' is just a label and does not affect reality. His response is that the reality of eternal punishment is what matters, and that punishment makes the major sinner equivalent to a disbeliever in terms of consequence, even if the name is disputed.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the Quranic citations and the flow from division to conclusion to objection to response.
The passage contains several abbreviated verse references marked as 'الآية'.
2
Identify the Quranic divisions
List the verses Maturidi uses to show that Allah divides people into only two groups (believer/disbeliever). Pay attention to how he interprets each one.
For example, verse 64:2 explicitly says 'among you is the disbeliever and among you is the believer'.
3
Understand the conclusion about major sins
Notice how Maturidi moves from the division to the threat of eternal fire for disbelievers, then concludes that the major sinner (who is threatened similarly) must be a disbeliever.
This is his core argument: the threat implies the category.
4
Analyze the objection and response
The objection claims that names like 'disbeliever' are irrelevant; only the underlying reality matters. Maturidi responds that even if the name is not applied, the person suffers the same punishment, so the reality is what counts. He also argues that despair of mercy (a characteristic of disbelievers) applies.
Maturidi does not yield on the name; he says it is not prevented because the punishment is the same.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع ما قسم الله البشر الذين جرى عليهم القلم فيما عليه أمرهم في الدنيا والآخرة، فقال: «هو الذي خلقكم فمنكم كافر ومنكم مؤمن» [التغابن: 2]، وقال: «فمن شاء فليؤمن ومن شاء فليكفر» [الكهف: 29]، وقال: «فمن يرد الله أن يهديه» الآية، وقال: «ويضل من يشاء» الآية، وقال: «أفمن كان مؤمنا كمن كان فاسقاً لا يستوون» [السجدة: 18]، ثم بين كفر المسمى فاسقاً. وقال في أمر الآخرة: «يوم تبيض وجوه» الآية، وقال: «فأما من أوتي كتابه بيمينه» الآية، فجعلهم جميعا قسمين فلا ثالث في التحقيق مع ما بين أن النار أعدت للكافرين. فإذا ثبت الوعيد لصاحب الكبيرة لزم جعله كافرا. وبعد، فإن الله تعالى وصف أنه لا ييأس من روح الله إلا القوم الكافرون، وقد لزم الإياس على قول هؤلاء إن لم يلزمه اسم الكفر. على أن الأسماء لا منافع لها ولا مضار بها على أهلها، إنما المضار والنافع في حقائق ما لها الأسماء. فإذا لزم الخلود في النار بطلت فائدة الاسم إن كان مؤمناً أو كافرًا ولكن لا يمنع عنه اسم الكفر إذ عوقب بعقوبته. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Along with what Allah has divided of mankind—upon whom the Pen has passed regarding their affair in this world and the Hereafter—for He said: 'It is He who created you, and among you is the disbeliever and among you is the believer' [At-Taghabun 64:2], and He said: 'So whoever wills – let him believe; and whoever wills – let him disbelieve' [Al-Kahf 18:29], and He said: 'For whoever Allah wills to guide'—the verse, and He said: 'And He misguides whom He wills'—the verse, and He said: 'Then is one who was a believer like one who was a defiantly disobedient? They are not equal' [As-Sajdah 32:18], then He made clear the disbelief of the one called fasiq. And He said concerning the matter of the Hereafter: 'On the Day [when] faces will become white'—the verse, and He said: 'Then as for he who is given his record in his right hand'—the verse, so He made them all two divisions, with no third in reality—along with what He made clear that the Fire is prepared for the disbelievers. So if the threat is established for the perpetrator of a major sin, then it necessitates making him a disbeliever. Moreover, Allah described that none despairs of the mercy of Allah except the disbelieving people, and despair has become necessary according to the saying of these if the name of disbelief does not apply to him. However, names have no benefit or harm for their owners; benefit and harm are in the realities denoted by the names. So if eternal stay in the Fire is necessary, the benefit of the name is nullified, whether he is a believer or a disbeliever—but the name of disbelief is not prevented from him since he is punished with its punishment. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage continues the argument that the major sinner must be a disbeliever. Maturidi cites several Qur'anic verses that divide people into only two categories: believer and disbeliever. He points out that in Surah As-Sajdah, Allah explicitly contrasts the believer with the fasiq and then clarifies the disbelief of the one called fasiq. He also references the division of people on Judgment Day into two groups. If the Fire is prepared for disbelievers and the major sinner is threatened with it, then he must be a disbeliever. Then Maturidi addresses an objection: some might say that the threat is meant for those who consider sin lawful or that names are insignificant. He responds by noting that even if the name 'disbeliever' is not applied, the despair of Allah's mercy—a characteristic of disbelievers—applies to such a person if he despairs. Ultimately, he argues that names themselves carry no intrinsic harm or benefit; what matters is the reality (the punishment). If a major sinner is condemned to eternal fire, it is as if he is a disbeliever in terms of punishment, even if the name is withheld. However, the name is not prevented because he suffers the same punishment. So the conclusion is that the major sinner is a disbeliever.

Correction: Maturidi is not arguing that every sin makes one a disbeliever; rather, he argues from specific Quranic divisions and threat verses that major sins that incur the threat of eternal fire place one in the category of disbelievers in terms of ultimate outcome, though he may still consider them believers in some sense regarding the name, but the punishment is the same.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that because the Quran divides people into only believers and disbelievers, and major sinners are threatened with eternal fire, they must be considered disbelievers; the name is secondary to the reality of punishment.

Eternal Threat Necessitates Naming Major Sinners as Kafirs
Maturidi argues from Quranic verses that the threat of hell for major sins necessitates calling the sinner a kafir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the Quran's threat of eternal punishment for major sins logically requires considering the major sinner as a disbeliever in terms of the divine threat.

Core Idea
Eternal threat logically entails calling the major sinner a kafir.

Maturidi uses several Quranic verses to establish that the Fire is prepared for disbelievers and that people are divided into two groups. He then argues that if the threat of eternal punishment is established for the major sinner, it necessitates calling him a kafir, because the reality of punishment overrides the name.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verses
Read the verses Maturidi quotes, noting how they divide people into two categories (believers and disbelievers) and state that the Fire is prepared for disbelievers.
These verses set the foundation for the argument.
2
Identify the logical step
Maturidi states: 'فإذا ثبت الوعيد لصاحب الكبيرة لزم جعله كافرا' – if the threat of eternal punishment is established for the major sinner, it necessitates making him a kafir. Understand this as a logical consequence.
3
Consider the objection and response
Some may argue that despair of mercy does not force the name kufr. Maturidi responds that names have no inherent benefit or harm; only the reality matters. So even if the name is not applied, the punishment remains.
This shows the argument is about the reality of divine threat, not worldly legal rulings.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that the eternal punishment nullifies the benefit of any name, so the major sinner is effectively treated as a kafir in terms of divine threat.
The lesson is that divine categories are based on ultimate consequences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال: فمن شاء فليؤمن ومن شاء فليكفر، وقال: فمن يرد الله أن يهديه الآية، وقال: ويضل من يشاء الآية، وقال: أفمن كان مؤمنا كمن كان فاسقا لا يستوون، ثم بين كفر المسمى فاسقا. وقال في أمر الآخرة: يوم تبيض وجوه الآية، وقال: فأما من أوتي كتابه بيمينه الآية، فجعلهم جميعا قسمين فلا ثالث في التحقيق مع ما بين أن النار أعدت للكافرين. فإذا ثبت الوعيد لصاحب الكبيرة لزم جعله كافرا. وبعد، فإن الله تعالى وصف أنه لا ييأس من روح الله إلا القوم الكافرون، وقد لزم الإياس على قول هؤلاء لم يلزمه اسم الكفر. على أن الأسماء لا منافع لها ولا مضار بها على أهلها، إنما المضار والنافع في حقائق ما لها الأسماء. فإذا لزم الخلود في النار بطلت فائدة الاسم إن كان مؤمنا أو كافرا لا يمنع عنه اسم الكفر إذ عوقب بعقوبته. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And He said: 'Then whoever wills, let him believe; and whoever wills, let him disbelieve' (al-Kahf 18:29). And He said: 'For whoever Allah intends to guide, He expands his breast to Islam' (al-An'am 6:125), and He said: 'And He sends astray whom He wills' (al-Nahl 16:93). And He said: 'Is one who was a believer like one who was a rebellious sinner? They are not equal' (al-Sajda 32:18). Then He explained the disbelief of the one called a rebellious sinner. And He said concerning the matter of the Hereafter: 'On the Day when faces become white' (Al Imran 3:106), and He said: 'Then as for he who is given his book in his right hand' (al-Haqqa 69:19). Afterwards, Allah the Exalted described that none despairs of the Spirit of Allah except the disbelieving people (Surah Yusuf 12:87). Despair would be necessary according to the saying of these people; it does not necessitate for him the name of disbelief. But names have no benefits and no harms to their possessors; only the harms and benefits are in the realities for which the names exist. So if eternal (khulud) stay in the Fire is necessitated, the benefit of the name is nullified whether he is a believer or a disbeliever; the name of disbelief does not prevent it since he is punished with its punishment. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 421

Commentary
This passage is a proof from Quranic verses that the threat of eternal punishment for major sins logically requires calling the perpetrator a kafir. The author first quotes several verses that divide people into two categories: believers and disbelievers, and that the Fire is prepared for disbelievers. He then argues: if the threat (al-wa'id) of hellfire is established for the major sinner, then it necessitates making him a kafir. He anticipates an objection: some might say that despair (al-ya's) of Allah's mercy would be necessary according to their view, but that does not necessitate the name of kufr. He responds by stating that names themselves have no inherent benefit or harm; the real harm is in the reality of punishment. Therefore, even if the name kafir is not applied, the eternal punishment remains, and the name does not prevent that punishment. He concludes that there is no power except with Allah.

Correction: Maturidi is arguing from the logical consequence of the divine threat: the eternal punishment necessitates that in terms of ultimate outcome, the major sinner is like a disbeliever, but he is not necessarily applying the legal name of kufr in this life.

The Hadith on Hypocrisy and Major Sinners
Maturidi cites the hadith on hypocrisy and links it to major sins.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi uses the hadith on the three signs of hypocrisy to argue that these traits are evident in those who commit major sins.

Core Idea
Major sins manifest the traits of hypocrisy.

In this short but dense passage, Imam Maturidi cites a well-known hadith about the signs of hypocrisy and immediately applies it to the question of major sinners. He argues that the same traits that mark a hypocrite—lying, breaking promises, betraying trusts—are evident in those who commit major sins. This sets the stage for a deeper theological discussion on the relationship between faith and sin.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal English translation. Notice the flow from the opening statement to the hadith citation to Maturidi's application.
The passage begins with a general comment about truth and falsehood being tested.
2
Identify the three signs
The hadith lists three traits that mark a hypocrite. Locate each one in Arabic and English. Notice the verbs used: 'speaks lies', 'breaks promises', 'betrays trusts'.
These three traits are described as signs of hypocrisy in the well-known tradition.
3
Reflect on Maturidi's application
Maturidi states that all of these traits 'appear from the committer of a major sin'. Think about why he makes this connection. What does this imply about the relationship between major sins and hypocrisy?
This connection is crucial in Maturidi's broader theology of faith and sin.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بيان ما أعطته الألسن من الصدق والكذب بالمحنة. وكذا روى عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم أنه قال: ثلاث من كن فيه فهو منافق من إذا حدث كذب، وإذا وعد أخلف، وإذا اؤتمن خان. فقد ظهر له ذلك كله من مرتكب الكبيرة. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Statement of what tongues give of truthfulness and falsehood through trial. And likewise it is narrated from the Messenger of Allah (peace be upon him) that he said: 'Three [traits] whoever possesses them is a hypocrite: when he speaks he lies, when he promises he breaks, and when he is entrusted he betrays.' So all of that appears from the committer of a major sin. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage begins with a statement about how truthfulness and falsehood are tested through trial (al-mihna). It then cites the well-known hadith from the Prophet ﷺ listing three signs of a hypocrite: lying when speaking, breaking promises, and betraying trusts. The author, Imam Maturidi, connects this to the committer of a major sin (murtakib al-kabirah), asserting that these traits are evident in such a person. This sets up the broader discussion on the status of faith in someone who commits major sins.

The Mu'tazilah Naming of the Major Sinner
Maturidi records the Mu'tazilah argument on the name of the major sinner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilah argument for why the major sinner cannot be called a believer or a disbeliever, but rather a transgressor (fasiq).

Core Idea
The major sinner is neither believer nor disbeliever but a fasiq.

The Mu'tazilah argued that the major sinner cannot be called a believer because faith is a good name, and the major sinner is associated with evil names. They also noted that the promise of reward is attached to the name 'believer' and is general, so the threat of punishment excludes the major sinner from being a believer. However, since revelation does not call him a disbeliever, they placed him in a middle category: fasiq.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow of the argument.
The argument is compact; take your time.
2
Understand the naming logic
Identify why the major sinner cannot be called a believer (evil names, promise attached to faith) and why he is not called a disbeliever (no revelation).
The Mu'tazilah use a via media.
3
Identify the three names given
The passage concludes that the major sinner is called fasiq, fajir, and zalim. Reflect on why these names are appropriate.
These terms imply moral corruption.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واحتجت المعتزلة في الاسم بما سُمّي صاحب الكبيرة بأسماء خبيثة، والإيمان من الأسماء الطيبة لا يسمّى بها. مع ما جاء من الوعد باسم الإيمان، والوعد لا يحتمل الخصوص؛ ثم صاحب الكبيرة قد جاء فيه الوعيد فبطل أن يكون مؤمنا ولم يُسم به كافرًا بما لم يرد به التسمية، فسمّي بالذي أجمع أنه له اسم وهو الفسق والفجور والظلم. ثم لهم في الوعيد أمران: عموم أخباره، والثاني قوله: إن تجتنبوا كبائر ما تنهون عنه الآية، بين ما لا يغفر ويغفر. مع ما كان الوعيد بالتخليد أعظم في المنع وأبلغ في الزجر، فهو أحق. على أن الوعيد إذا وجب لزم دخول النار، ولم يذكر فيهم الخروج ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And the Mu'tazilah argued concerning the name: because the possessor of a major sin is called by evil names, and faith is among the good names, so he is not called by it. Moreover, the promise came with the name of faith, and the promise does not admit specification; then the possessor of a major sin has the threat (al-wa'id) come upon him, so it becomes invalid that he be a believer, and he is not called a disbeliever because the designation has not come for it; so he is called by what it is agreed that he has a name, namely transgression (fisq), immorality (fujur), and injustice (zulm). Then they have two matters concerning the threat: the generality of its reports, and the second is His statement: 'If you avoid the major sins you are forbidden...' the verse, showing what is not forgiven and what is forgiven. Moreover, the threat of eternal [punishment] is greater in prevention and more effective in deterrence, so it is more appropriate. Yet if the threat is necessitated, entering the fire becomes necessary, and no exit is mentioned for them. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage presents the Mu'tazilah argument on the status of the major sinner. They argue that such a person cannot be called a believer because faith is a good name and the major sinner is associated with evil names. They also argue that the promise (al-wa'd) is connected to the name 'believer' and is general, not specific. Since the threat of punishment (al-wa'id) applies to the major sinner, he is not a believer, but he is also not called a disbeliever (kafir) because that designation is not applied to him by revelation. Instead, he is called by names like fasiq, fajir, and zalim. They then present two points about the threat: first, the general nature of the reports on threat, and second, the verse 'If you avoid the major sins...' which indicates forgiveness for minor sins. They also stress that the threat of eternal punishment is a greater deterrent. Ultimately, if the threat is necessitated, the sinner must enter the fire and no exit is mentioned for them.

Key Terms

وعد
فسق
فجور
ظلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Mu'tazilah consider the major sinner to be a disbeliever.
Correction: They actually place him in an intermediate category: fasiq, neither believer nor disbeliever.

Checkpoint

A) Believer (mu'min)
B) Disbeliever (kafir)
C) Transgressor (fasiq)
D) Hypocrite (munafiq)

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilah argued that the major sinner is neither believer nor disbeliever but a fasiq.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a major sin, do you immediately justify it by telling yourself you are still a believer, or do you let the weight of calling it 'fisq' (transgression) drive you to repentance? Which label—iman or fisq—do you truly deserve in that moment, and what must you do to reclaim the name of faith?
?
The Mu'tazilah argued that a believer is described by good names, while the major sinner is called by evil ones. Look at your own life: are there any areas where your actions earn you the name of fasiq (transgressor) rather than mu'min? What would it take for you to remove that name and fully embody iman?

The Mu'tazilah Argument on the Threat of Punishment
Maturidi presents the Mu'tazilah arguments on the threat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilah two points on the threat (wa'id) and their conclusion that the major sinner must enter the fire eternally.

Core Idea
The threat of punishment for major sins is general and implies eternal fire with no exit.

The Mu'tazilah present two arguments concerning the threat of punishment. First, the reports about threat are general and include all major sinners. Second, the verse about avoiding major sins indicates that only minor sins may be forgiven, implying that major sins are not forgiven without punishment. They also argue that the threat of eternal punishment is a greater deterrent, and if the threat is established, the sinner must enter the fire and no exit is mentioned.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Pay attention to the two matters enumerated.
The phrase 'في الوعيد أمران' signals the structure.
2
Identify the two matters
First matter: generality of the reports of threat. Second matter: the verse showing what is forgiven and what is not.
These are the core evidences.
3
Understand the conclusion
The Mu'tazilah conclude that entering the fire is necessary with no exit mentioned.
This reinforces their doctrine of eternal punishment for major sinners.

Maturidi on the Scope of the Threat (Wa'id)
Maturidi analyzes agreement and disagreement on the threat (al-wa'id).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the points of agreement and disagreement between the Mu'tazilah, Khawarij, and Murji'ah regarding the divine threat of punishment for sin, as presented by Imam Maturidi.

Core Idea
All parties agree the threat excludes believers, but differ on its application to sinful believers.

Imam Maturidi engages with the Mu'tazilah argument about the threat (al-wa'id) by examining the positions of various theological groups. He uncovers a surprising consensus: despite their differences, all agree that the threat does not apply to believers who remain within faith. Yet they diverge on how to treat believers who commit sins. This lesson unpacks Maturidi's nuanced analysis.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how Maturidi uses 'we say' and 'with Allah we seek help' as a formula.
2
Identify the consensus
Maturidi states that all groups agree on a specific point about the threat. Find that point and understand the condition he attaches to it.
The condition is 'every sin that expels its doer from faith'.
3
Compare the positions
Contrast the Murji'ah position with that of the Mu'tazilah and Khawarij. What do they agree on? Where do they differ?
The Murji'ah fear punishment for sinful believers, while the others do not.
4
Reflect on the implication
Maturidi concludes that the Murji'ah apply the threat more broadly. Why does he say that? Consider how each group limits or extends the scope of threat reports.
The other groups restrict the threat to non-believers only.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الفقيه أبو منصور رحمه الله: نقول وبالله نستعين: أجمع هؤلاء على اختلافهم على أن الوعيد مما لم يُشرك فيه المؤمنون، بل هو في كل ذنب أخرج صاحبه عن الإيمان وأسقط عنه اسمه. والمرجئة توافقهم على أن كل ذنب يُخرج صاحبه عن الإيمان فالوعيد له لازم. ثم إن المرجئة تخاف على المؤمنين فيما ارتكبوا من المأثم مع قيام الإيمان بالعقوبة، وأولئك لا يخافون عليهم؛ وكان احتجاجهم بعموم الآثار فثبت بالذي ذكرت من قول الجملة أن المرجئة، وهي التي أرجأت الذنوب، أشد استعمالاً لها على العموم من الذين ادعوا عمومها، إذ هم عند التحصيل جعلوا الوعيد في أحد فريقي البشر، وهم الذين ليسوا بمؤمنين. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Al-Faqih Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: We say, and with Allah we seek help: These groups, despite their differences, have agreed that the threat (al-wa'id) is something in which believers are not included, rather it is in every sin that expels its doer from faith and removes his name. And the Murji'ah agree with them that every sin that expels its doer from faith, the threat is necessary for him. Then, indeed, the Murji'ah fear for the believers regarding the sins they committed while faith remains concerning punishment, and those (i.e., the Mu'tazilah and Khawarij) do not fear for them. And their argument was based on the generality of the reports; so it is established by what I mentioned from the statement of the group that the Murji'ah—who postpone (arja'at) sins—are more severe in applying them generally than those who claimed their generality, because when analyzed, they placed the threat only in one of the two categories of mankind, namely those who are not believers. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage is Imam Maturidi's response to the Mu'tazilah argument. He begins by stating a consensus among the groups (including Mu'tazilah, Khawarij, and Murji'ah) that the threat (al-wa'id) does not include believers; rather, it applies only to sins that expel a person from faith and remove the name of faith from him. He then explains that the Murji'ah agree on this point: any sin that removes from faith necessitates the threat. However, the Murji'ah differ in that they fear punishment for believers who commit sins while retaining faith, whereas the other groups do not fear for such believers. Maturidi points out that the Murji'ah, by postponing judgment on sins, actually apply the threat reports more broadly than the Mu'tazilah and Khawarij, because the latter restrict the threat to only non-believers. In essence, the Murji'ah maintain that the threat can apply to believers who sin, but they still affirm their faith.

Key Terms

مرجئة
إيمان
ذنب
الآثار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Murji'ah deny that sin has any consequences for believers.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the Murji'ah actually fear punishment for believers who sin, but they do not consider such sins to remove faith.

Checkpoint
On what point do the Mu'tazilah, Khawarij, and Murji'ah agree according to Maturidi?
A) The threat applies to every sin
B) The threat does not apply to believers
C) The threat applies only to sins that expel from faith
D) The threat is only for non-believers

Key Takeaway
Maturidi reveals that the Murji'ah, by fearing punishment for sinful believers, actually apply the threat reports more broadly than those who restrict it to non-believers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Maturidi shows that even the Murji'ah feared punishment for believers who sin. How often do you let the fear of Allah's threat (al-wa'id) govern your daily actions, or do you rely on your faith to protect you?
?
The threat is for those expelled from faith. What sin in your life might be gradually removing you from faith without you realizing? Examine yourself—are you walking the path of the Mu'tazilah by denying the threat to believers, or the path of the Murji'ah by fearing it?

Maturidi's Proof: Faith as Affirmation and Its Permanence
Maturidi proves faith is tasdiq and remains with the sinner.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam Maturidi uses Quran, consensus, and language to prove that faith is inner affirmation (tasdiq) and that it remains with a believer even after committing major sins.

Core Idea
Faith is affirmation of the heart, not mere works; it endures despite sin.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents a foundational argument for the definition of faith. He draws on Quranic proofs, the practice of the Muslim community, and linguistic usage to show that faith is essentially affirmation (tasdiq). He then uses a remarkable consensus argument—even his opponents inadvertently affirm that a sinner is still a believer—to prove that faith does not vanish with sin. Let's examine his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Understand the claim: faith is tasdiq
Maturidi asserts that faith is affirmation (al-tasdiq). This is not a mere utterance but a conviction of the heart. Notice how he bases this on Quranic proofs, the practice of believers, and linguistic usage.
Tasdiq is internal, unlike amal (action) or iqrar (confession).
2
Examine the evidence provided
He cites three sources: the Quran, the conduct of the faithful (such as participation in worship and gatherings), and the way language operates. Together they show that faith is the inner affirmation that underlies all religious acts.
3
Follow the consensus argument
Maturidi points out that even the Khawarij, Mu'tazila, and Hashwiyya do not deny that a major sinner is called a believer in common discourse. This implicit consensus proves that faith remains with the sinner.
This refutes the Khariji and Mu'tazili position that the major sinner becomes a kafir.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم قد ثبت بأدلة القرآن وما عليه أهل الإيمان والذي جرى به من اللسان أن الإيمان هو التصديق؛ به نؤمن، وبذلك جرت أحكام القرآن في الحلال والحرام، وما به قيام العبادات والاشتراك في الجماعات والاجتماع في مجالس الذكر والخيرات على غير تناكر منهم. القبول بحق المؤمنين وكذا جميع ما جرى به الخطاب؛ لم يوجد معتزلي ولا خارجي ولا حشوي - مع ما فيهم أنواع المعاصي والسيئات التي بان لهم أنها كبائر أو لم يبن لهم حقيقتها - يجيز في أمر الخطاب أن يكون هو نفسه غير أحد له ما فيه. فثبت أن الإيمان لم يزل عنه وأن الاسم قائم له فيبطل بهذه الجملة - التي من دفعها يُعلم أنه مكابر معاند - ما قالت الخوارج والمعتزلة. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Then it has been established by the proofs of the Quran and what the people of faith are upon and what has come by way of language that faith is affirmation (al-tasdiq); with it we believe, and by it the rulings of the Quran in the halal and haram operate, and by it acts of worship are established, participation in congregations, gathering in assemblies of remembrance and good deeds without denial among them. Acceptance of the rights of the believers, and likewise all that has come in discourse: there is no Mu'tazili, Khariji, or Hashwi—despite the types of sins and evil deeds among them, whether they know they are major or do not know their reality—who would permit in the matter of discourse that he himself be other than one who has [faith] in him. So it is established that faith has not ceased from him and that the name remains for him; and thus by this statement—whoever rejects it is known to be obstinate and stubborn—what the Khawarij and Mu'tazilah said is invalidated. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage presents a key proof from Imam Maturidi. He asserts that it is established by Quranic proofs, the practice of the people of faith, and linguistic usage that faith (iman) is affirmation (tasdiq). Through this affirmation, the rulings of the Quran regarding halal and haram operate, and acts of worship, community gatherings, and assemblies of remembrance are carried out without mutual denial. He argues that no one—neither Mu'tazili, Khariji, nor Hashwi—would accept that the person who participates in these acts of faith is not truly a believer, despite the sins they commit. Therefore, faith does not leave the major sinner, and the name 'believer' remains applicable to him. This refutes the position of the Khawarij and Mu'tazilah who claim that the major sinner is not a believer.

Key Terms
التصديق
إيمان
مؤمن
الكبائر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Faith is only outward utterance or is nullified by major sins.
Correction: Faith is inner affirmation (tasdiq) that remains with the believer even after committing major sins, as proven by Quran, practice, language, and consensus.

Checkpoint
What is the definition of faith given in this passage?
A) Utterance of the tongue
B) Action of the limbs
C) Affirmation (tasdiq)
D) Both belief and action

Key Takeaway
Faith is inner affirmation (tasdiq) that endures even after major sins, a key Maturidi doctrine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you ever find yourself looking at a fellow Muslim who commits major sins and doubting their faith? How does Maturidi's argument challenge that tendency within you? Will you now actively fight that thought?
?
If faith is merely affirmation of the heart that remains even with sin, what does that mean for the state of your own heart? Is your inner affirmation strong enough to carry you through your own sins? What can you do today to strengthen that core affirmation?
?
The fact that even those with heretical views do not deny the name of believer to sinners in ordinary discourse——does your own speech reflect that reality? Are you quick to label someone as less of a believer because of their sin? What step will you take to align your speech with the truth of this lesson?

Quranic Evidence that Believers Retain Faith Despite Major Sins
Maturidi cites two Quranic verses to prove the name of faith remains with sinning believers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses Quranic verses to demonstrate that believers who commit major sins are still called believers.

Core Idea
Quranic rebuke and conflict among believers do not strip them of the name of faith.

Maturidi continues his argument by turning to the Quran itself. He presents two verses that address believers in the midst of sin or dispute, showing that the title 'believer' remains despite wrongdoing. This passage grounds the theological position in scriptural evidence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two verses
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that Maturidi cites two verses: one from Surah al-Saff and one from Surah al-Hujurat. Both address sinners as believers.
The passage ends mid-sentence, but the argument is clear.
2
Analyse the first evidence: verse 61:2
In 'O you who have believed, why do you say what you do not do?', Allah uses the name of faith while rebuking. The rebuke (maqt) is attached to believers before they commit the sin (since 'why do you say' refers to a statement). This shows the name remains.
The word 'maqt' does not imply expulsion from faith.
3
Analyse the second evidence: verse 49:9
Allah addresses fighting parties as 'believers' and commands support for the oppressed. Even though one party is a transgressor (baghi), the name of faith is not removed. If fighting caused loss of faith, they would not be called believers.
The verse implies that transgression does not negate faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً إن الله سبحانه أبقى له اسم الإيمان مع تحقيق ما عليه الوعيد في حكمه بقوله: يا أيها الذين آمنوا لم تقولون ما لا تفعلون الآية، فأوجب فيه المقت عنده مع اسم الإيمان بحرف العتاب الذي لا يحتمل النطق قبل مقارفة الذنب بقوله: ولم تقولون، والمقت لا يوجب الذنب الذي في الحكمة لزوم المغفرة له. وقال: وإن طائفتان من المؤمنين اقتتلوا، أثبت لهم اسم الإيمان مع إلزام اسم البغي لأحدهما في القتال، وألزم من حضر معونة المبغي عليه حتى يرجع الآخر إلى أمر الله؛ ولو كان ذلك خروج من الإيمان.
And also, Allah—glorified is He—preserved for him the name of faith while actualizing the threat upon him in His judgment by His statement: 'O you who have believed, why do you say what you do not do?' (the verse), so He made indignation (al-maqt) necessary for him with the name of faith through the letter of rebuke that does not admit utterance before committing the sin in His saying: 'Why do you say?' And indignation does not necessitate the sin which in wisdom necessitates forgiveness for it. And He said: 'And if two parties among the believers fight'—He affirmed for them the name of faith while requiring the name of transgression (al-baghy) for one of them in the fighting, and He required those present to support the oppressed party until the other returns to the command of Allah; and if that were expulsion from faith...
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage provides additional Quranic evidence that the name of faith remains with believers even when they commit major sins. First, Maturidi cites the verse 'O you who have believed, why do you say what you do not do?' (Surah al-Saff, 61:2). Here Allah addresses them as 'believers' while rebuking them for saying what they do not do. The rebuke (al-maqt) is attached to them with the name of faith, and this rebuke occurs before the sin is committed (since 'why do you say' implies a statement before action). This shows that faith remains. Second, the verse 'And if two parties among the believers fight' (Surah al-Hujurat, 49:9) affirms the name of believers for both parties despite one being a transgressor (baghi). Allah commands support for the oppressed party until the other returns to His command. If fighting were a cause of expulsion from faith, the name would not be given. The passage ends mid-sentence, but the conclusion is clear: the name of faith remains.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: When Allah rebukes a believer or calls them to account, it means they have lost faith.
Correction: The Quran continues to address them as believers, showing the name remains even with rebuke. Rebuke is not expulsion from faith.

Key Takeaway
The Quran preserves the title 'believer' for those who sin, proving faith is not lost by major sins.

Which rebuke from Quran strikes closest to your own life? Are you saying what you do not do? How will you align your actions with your words today, knowing you are still called a believer?

Faith and Disbelief
intermediate
5 min
The Name of Faith Remains with Sinners: Foundational Argument
Maturidi gives a foundational argument on the persistence of faith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's foundational argument that the name of faith (īmān) persists even after major sins, based on the definition of faith as affirmation and the practical consensus of all groups in addressing sinners as believers.

Core Idea
Faith is affirmation, and no group denies calling a sinner a believer in practice.

Before diving into the Quranic verses, Maturidi establishes a key principle: the very definition of faith and the way the Qur'an addresses Muslims demonstrate that the name of faith cannot be stripped away by sin. Even the Khawarij and Mu'tazila—who theoretically deny this—unwittingly affirm it in their own use of language.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening argument
Read the Arabic passage from the beginning through 'فثبت أن الإيمان لم يزل عنه'. This sets up the claim that the name of faith remains.
Notice that Maturidi first refutes the opponent's claim about the threat (wa'īd) being exclusive to non-believers.
2
Understand the definition of faith
Maturidi states that faith is taṣdīq (affirmation). Reflect on how this definition allows for sins without negating faith.
This is the cornerstone: if faith is merely affirmation, actions don't define it.
3
Recognize the practical consensus
Maturidi points out that even the opposing sects, despite their sins, still use the term 'believer' for sinners in their own address. This undermines their theoretical position.
The argument is that if they truly believed sin removes faith, they would avoid calling a sinner a believer.
4
Identify the conclusion
The passage concludes that the name of faith remains, invalidating the Khawarij and Mu'tazila positions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ادعوا عمومها، إذ هم عند التحصيل جعلوا الوعيد في أحد فريقي البشر، وهم الذين ليسوا بمؤمنين. ولا قوة إلا بالله . ثم قد ثبت بأدلة القرآن وما عليه أهل الإيمان والذي جرى به من اللسان أن الإيمان هو التصديق؛ به نؤمن، وبذلك جرت أحكام القرآن في الحلال والحرام، وما به قيام العبادات والاشتراك في الجماعات والاجتماع في مجالس الذكر والخيرات على غير تناكر منهم . القبول بحق المؤمنين وكذا جميع ما جرى به الخطاب؛ لم يوجد معتزلي ولا خارجي ولا حشوي [حشوية؟] - مع ما فيهم أنواع المعاصي والسيئات التي بان لهم أنها كبائر أو لم يبن لهم حقيقتها - يجيز في أمر الخطاب أن يكون [ هو نفسه ] غير أحد له ما فيه . فثبت أن الإيمان لم يزل عنه وأن الاسم قائم له فيبطل بهذه الجملة - التي من دفعها يُعلم أنه مكابر معاند ـ ما قالت الخوارج والمعتزلة. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وأيضاً إن الله سبحانه أبقى له اسم الإيمان مع تحقيق ما عليه الوعيد في حكمه بقوله : يا أيها الذين آمنوا لم تقولون ما لا تفعلون [الصف، ٢/٦١-٣]، فأوجب فيه المقت عنده مع اسم الإيمان بحرف العتاب الذي لا يحتمل النطق قبل مقارفة الذنب بقوله : ولم تقولون ، والمقت لا يوجب الذنب الذي في الحكمة لزوم المغفرة له. وقال: وإن طائفتان من المؤمنين اقتتلوا [الحجرات، ٩/٤٩]، أثبت لهم اسم الإيمان مع إلزام اسم البغي لأحدهما في القتال، وألزم من حضر معونة المبغي عليه حتى يرجع الآخر إلى أمر الله ؛ ولو كان ذلك خروجا من الإيمان لكان الحق في مثل ذلك غير الذي ذكر. وقال: يا أيها الذين آمنوا كتب عليكم القصاص في القتلى [البقرة، ۱۷۸/٢]، ومعلوم أنه لا يجب إلا بقتل العمد فأثبت لهم في ابتداء الآية اسم الإيمان، وأبقى بينهما الأخوة ، وأخبر أن ذلك تخفيف من ربكم ورحمة، وتبعد هذه الأوصاف فيمن أخرجهم الفعل من الإيمان. وقال: والذين آمنوا ولم يهاجروا ما لكم من ولايتهم من شيء [الأنفال، ٧٢/٨]، ثم قال : وإن استنصروكم في الدين الآية، أثبت لهم اسم الإيمان وجمع بينهم في الدين على تخلفهم عن الهجرة، مع عظم ما فيه من الوعيد بقوله: الذين توفاهم الملائكة ظالمي أنفسهم [النساء، ٩٧/٤] الآية، وقال : يا أيها الذين آمنوا لا تتخذوا عدوي وعدوكم أولياء [الممتحنة، ١/٦٠] الآية، وقال أيضا: لا تخونوا الله والرسول [الأنفال، ٢٧/٨] الآية، فأثبت لهم اسم الإيمان مع قبح صنيعهم. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وأيضاً إن الله تعالى قال: يا أيها الذين آمنوا توبوا إلى الله توبة نصوحا [التحريم، ٨/٦٦]، وقال : وتوبوا إلى الله جميعا أيها المؤمنون [النور، ٣١/٢٤]، أخبر أن عليهم ذنوبا تغفر بالتوبة ويكفّر بها على إبقاء اسم الإيمان، وفي قول هؤلاء لا يجوز ذلك. فثبت أن القول [الحق] هو قول من
They alleged generality, while they in fact made the threat (al-wa'īd) apply to one of the two groups of mankind, those who are not believers. There is no power except with Allah. Then it has been established by the proofs of the Qur'an, and what the people of faith hold, and what has proceeded from the tongue, that faith (al-īmān) is affirmation (al-taṣdīq); with it we believe, and by it the rulings of the Qur'an proceed in lawful and unlawful, and by it the acts of worship are established, and participation in congregations, and gathering in assemblies of remembrance and good deeds without mutual denial among them. Acceptance of the right of the believers, and likewise all that has proceeded in address; there is not found a Mu'tazilī, nor a Khārijī, nor a Ḥashwī — despite their containing types of sins and evil deeds which they know are major sins or which they do not know their reality — who permits in the matter of address that he himself be other than one who has what is in it [of the status]. So it stands established that faith does not leave him and that the name stands for him. Thus by this statement — whoever rejects it is known to be an obstinate antagonist — what the Khawārij and Mu'tazila said is invalidated. There is no power except with Allah. And also, Allah the Exalted preserved for him the name of faith along with the realization of what the threat entails in His judgment by His saying: 'O you who have believed, why do you say what you do not do?' [al-Ṣaff 61:2-3]. So He made anger [arise] against him with the name of faith by the word of rebuke that does not permit utterance before the commission of the sin by His saying: 'Why do you say?' And anger does not necessitate the sin for which, in wisdom, forgiveness becomes necessary. And He said: 'And if two factions among the believers fight' [al-Ḥujurāt 49:9]. He affirmed for them the name of faith while imposing the name of aggression (al-baghy) on one of them in fighting, and obligated those present to assist the wronged one until the other returns to the command of Allah. And if that had been an exit from faith, the truth in such a case would be other than what was mentioned. And He said: 'O you who have believed, prescribed for you is legal retribution in cases of murder' [al-Baqarah 2:178]. And it is known that it is only obligatory for intentional killing. So He affirmed for them at the beginning of the verse the name of faith, and preserved brotherhood between them, and informed that that is a lightening from your Lord and mercy. And these descriptions would be far from those whose action excludes them from faith. And He said: 'And those who believed and did not emigrate — you have no guardianship of them in anything' [al-Anfāl 8:72], then He said: 'But if they seek help from you in the religion' — the verse. He affirmed for them the name of faith and united them in religion despite their failure to emigrate, along with the greatness of the threat in it by His saying: 'Those whom the angels take in death while they are wronging themselves' [al-Nisā' 4:97] — the verse. And He said: 'O you who have believed, do not take My enemy and your enemy as allies' [al-Mumtaḥanah 60:1] — the verse. And He also said: 'Do not betray Allah and the Messenger' [al-Anfāl 8:27] — the verse. So He affirmed for them the name of faith despite the evil of their deed. There is no power except with Allah. And also, Allah the Exalted said: 'O you who have believed, repent to Allah with sincere repentance' [al-Taḥrīm 66:8], and said: 'And repent to Allah, all of you, O believers' [al-Nūr 24:31]. He informed that they have sins that are forgiven by repentance and expiated while preserving the name of faith. And according to the saying of these [opponents], that is not permissible. So it stands established that the true saying is the saying of those who...
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage is a proof that the name of faith (īmān) remains with Muslims even when they commit major sins, refuting the Khawārij and Mu'tazila who held that grave sinners are no longer believers. The author begins by noting that the opponents (Khawārij/Mu'tazila) wrongly confine the threat of punishment to one group—non-believers—while they themselves agree that the term 'believer' is used for sinners in the Qur'anic address. He then establishes that the definition of faith is affirmation (taṣdīq), and that the Qur'anic rulings and communal practices are based on this. No sect denies that a sinner can be called a believer in Qur'anic discourse; even the Khawārij and Mu'tazila admit this in practice, though they theoretically deny it. Therefore, the claim that sin removes the name of faith is invalid. Next, Maturidi presents a series of Qur'anic verses that address sinners as 'O you who have believed' and yet rebuke or command them: 1) Sūrat al-Ṣaff 61:2-3 rebukes believers for saying what they do not do, yet they are called believers; the anger (maqt) mentioned does not necessitate that sin removes faith. 2) Sūrat al-Ḥujurāt 49:9 speaks of two parties of believers fighting, one being aggressors; they remain believers despite the aggression. If fighting removed faith, the ruling would be different. 3) Sūrat al-Baqarah 2:178 prescribes retribution for murder, addressing the believers; intentional killing is a major sin, but they are addressed as believers, and brotherhood is preserved. 4) Sūrat al-Anfāl 8:72 addresses believers who did not emigrate, with a threat for those who die in that state; yet they are still called believers. 5) Sūrat al-Mumtaḥanah 60:1 forbids taking enemies as allies, addressing believers who might commit this sin. 6) Sūrat al-Anfāl 8:27 forbids betraying Allah and the Messenger, again addressing believers. 7) Sūrat al-Taḥrīm 66:8 and Sūrat al-Nūr 24:31 command repentance to believers, implying they have sins that are forgiven while they retain the name of faith. In all these cases, the Qur'an affirms the name of faith despite the presence of sins, proving that sin does not remove one from faith. The conclusion is that the correct position is what the author's school holds: the name of faith remains with the sinner, contrary to the Khawārij and Mu'tazila.

تصديق

معتزلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The belief that any major sin expels a person from faith entirely.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the very definition of faith as affirmation, and the Qur'anic and communal practice of addressing sinners as believers, proves that sin does not remove the name of faith.

Key Takeaway
Faith is affirmation, and even sinners are still called believers in Qur'anic address, so major sins do not remove the name of faith.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If faith is simply affirmation, why do you sometimes feel that your sins diminish your faith? Examine your heart: do you truly affirm Allah's oneness and belief in the Prophet, or is there a gap between your tongue and your heart? Will you commit to closing that gap today?
?

So why do you sometimes deny the title of 'believer' to other Muslims who commit sins? Is fear of excusing sin making you harsh? Repent and resolve to uphold the brotherhood of believers, even when they fall short.
?
Maturidi argues based on how we address each other. In your daily interactions, do you subtly treat sinners as if they have left the faith? Let this lesson confront you: will you change your inner attitude and outward speech to reflect that a believer remains a believer, no matter the sin?

Quranic Proofs that Grave Sinners Remain Believers
Maturidi cites Quranic verses proving sinners retain faith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses multiple Quranic verses to demonstrate that the name of faith remains with believers even when they commit major sins.

Core Idea
Qur'an addresses sinners as 'O you who have believed' and commands them, preserving their faith.

Maturidi now presents a cascade of Qur'anic evidence: each verse addresses the faithful while rebuking or commanding them, proving that sin does not negate faith. From fighting and murder to betrayal and disobedience, the Qur'an consistently maintains the name of faith for the sinner.

Guided Path
1
Read the Quranic verses cited
Read from 'وأيضاً إن الله سبحانه أبقى له اسم الإيمان' to the end of the passage. Notice how each verse begins with 'O you who have believed' yet contains a rebuke or command involving sin.
Highlight the pattern: the address 'O you who have believed' preserves the name of faith despite the sin mentioned.
2
Analyze the first two verses
Focus on verses from Surah al-Saff (61:2-3) and Surah al-Hujurat (49:9). Maturidi explains how anger (maqt) and aggression (baghy) do not remove faith.
In al-Saff, the rebuke is for saying what one does not do, yet they are called believers. In al-Hujurat, fighters are called believers despite aggression.
3
Examine the remaining verses
Go through verses from al-Baqarah, al-Anfal, al-Mumtahanah, al-Tahrim, and al-Nur. Each one addresses believers who commit sins like murder, neglect of hijra, betrayal, and disobedience, yet the name of faith is retained.
Maturidi concludes that these verses prove the opponent's position is untenable.
4
Understand the conclusion
The passage ends by affirming that the true saying is that the name of faith remains, and the opposite view is invalid.
The lesson shows that repentance is commanded to believers, implying they have sins that need forgiveness.
